Demanding Correction of Transmission in Munazara
Author discusses the term 'objection to the claim' and opinions on requiring correction of transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on demanding correction of transmission and apply the rule according to the questioner's knowledge.

Core Idea
In munazara, objecting by demanding proof depends on whether you already know it's correct.

This lesson explores a key dialectical rule in munazara: when is it permissible or obligatory to demand that your opponent verify the transmission (naql) of a claim? The author introduces the term 'man' al-da'wa' and reports three scholarly positions. Understanding these distinctions prevents frivolous objections and ensures the debate proceeds on solid ground.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
The term 'man' al-da'wa' (objection to the claim) is used in munazara to refer to demanding correction of transmission. Note that this is a procedural objection, not a substantive one.
2
Analyze the three opinions
List the three opinions: obligatory (wajib), recommended (mustahsan), and conditional on the questioner's knowledge. Determine which scenario makes it obligatory and which makes it impermissible.
3
Apply the conditional opinion
Given a scenario: if the questioner is ignorant of the transmission's correctness, it is obligatory to demand correction. If he knows it is correct, it is not permissible. Why does this rule exist? Consider the balance between seeking truth and avoiding time-wasting.
4
Evaluate the implications
Reflect on how this rule prevents a debater from exploiting ignorance or wasting time. This is a practical tool for fair debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يطلق في هذا الفن على المطالبة بتصحيح النقل عبارة: (منع الدعوى). واختلفوا في طلب تصحيح النقل، فقيل: يجب، وقيل: يُستحسن، وقيل: إن كان (السائل) جاهلاً بصحة النقل، وجب طلب تصحيحه، وإن كان عالماً بأن النقل صحيح، لم يجز له طلب التصحيح.
And it may be termed in this art, regarding the demand for correction of transmission, the expression: 'objection to the claim'. And they differed concerning demanding correction of transmission: some said it is obligatory, some said it is recommended, and some said if the questioner is ignorant of the correctness of the transmission, then demanding its correction is obligatory; and if he knows that the transmission is correct, it is not permissible for him to demand correction.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the protocol of munazara. It introduces the term 'man' al-da'wa' (objection to the claim) which is used in this art to refer to demanding correction of transmission (naql). The author then reports a difference of opinion among scholars: some hold that demanding correction of transmission is obligatory (wajib), some say it is recommended (mustahsan), and a third opinion distinguishes based on the knowledge of the questioner (al-sa'il). If the questioner is ignorant of the correctness of the transmission, he must demand its correction; but if he knows it is correct, he is not allowed to demand correction. This nuanced view balances the need for sound evidence against the risk of frivolous objections.

Key Terms

تصحيح النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion mentioned, when is it obligatory for the questioner to demand correction of transmission?
A) When he is knowledgeable about its correctness.
B) When he is ignorant of its correctness.
C) When the opponent is lying.
D) Always obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The rule of demanding correction of transmission in munazara is not absolute; it depends on the questioner's knowledge, ensuring fairness and efficiency in debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you are a questioner (sa'il) in a munazara about whether napping invalidates wudu. Your opponent cites a weak hadith that you suspect is unreliable. According to the third opinion, what should you do? Explain your reasoning based on the passage.
?
Create a brief debate scenario involving two jurists arguing about a fiqh ruling. Identify a point where one could legitimately demand correction of transmission and another where it would be impermissible under the conditional opinion.

Rules for Objecting to and Defending Definitions in Dialectic
Author specifies the roles of participants and the valid objections and responses in definitional debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the four valid grounds for objecting to a definition and the method of taḥrīr al-murād in a dialectical context.

Core Idea
A valid objection to a definition must be one of four grounds; the defender uses clarification of intent to rebut.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), definitions are contested using strict rules. This passage outlines who the participants are, what objections are permissible, and how the defender may respond. Mastering these rules allows you to engage in rigorous definitional debate in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
Understand that the objector (mustadill) initiates the objection, and the defender (māni') responds.
In this text, mustadill is the objector, unlike other contexts.
2
Recognize the four valid objections
The objector can claim: (1) not exhaustive, (2) not exclusive, (3) entails impossibility (circularity, infinite regress, contradiction), (4) more obscure than the defined.
These correspond to the logical conditions for a sound definition.
3
Understand linguists' leniency
Linguists do not require verbal definitions to be comprehensive/exclusive, so objections 1 and 2 do not apply to them.
This shows the context-sensitivity of definitional rules.
4
Apply taḥrīr al-murād
When the objection is about comprehensiveness or exclusiveness, the defender clarifies his intent regarding the defined term, parts of the definition, or the underlying scholarly doctrine.
This technique rebuts objections by showing the objection misses the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضوابط المناظرة في التعريفات
أ ـ الذي يوجه الاعتراض على التعريف، يطلق عليه هنا اسم (مستدلّ)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (سائل). وصاحب التعريف الذي يدافع عن صحة تعريفه فيبين سقوط الاعتراض، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (مدافع).
ب - يُوجَّه الاعتراض على التعريف بما يخالف شروطه المنطقية، (فللمستدلّ) أن يعترض على التعريف بواحد من أمور أربعة :
١ - بكونه غير مطرد.
٢ - بكونه غير منعكس.
٣- بكونه يستلزم المحال كالدور السبقي، وكالتسلسل، وكاجتماع النقيضين .
٤ - بكونه أخفى من المعرَّف.
وعند من يتساهل في التعريف اللفظي كاللُّغويين، فلا يرون اشتراط كونه جامعاً مانعاً، لا يُوجَّه الاعتراض على تعريفاتهم اللفظية بأنها غير جامعة أو غير مانعة. فالمعروف أنهم لا يشترطون ذلك في بياناتهم للمفردات، وإنما يُعرِّفونها نوع تعريف، ويُميِّزونها نوع تمييز عن غيرها.
جـ - والأجوبة التي يدفع بها (المانع) صاحب التعريف، الاعتراضات التي يُوجِّهها (المستدل) على التعريف؛ ترجع إلى ما يلي :
أولاً : إذا كان الاعتراض الموجه على التعريف يتضمن كون التعريف غير جامع أو غير مانع، أي : غير مطرد أو غير منعكس، فالأجوبة ترجع إلى ما يطلقون عليه عبارة (تحرير المراد).
ويكون تحرير المراد هنا بأربعة وجوه :
الوجه الأول : تحرير المراد من المعرَّف.
الوجه الثاني : تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف.
الوجه الثالث : تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التعريف.
Rules of debate concerning definitions:
A - The one who directs the objection against the definition is called here (mustadill), and some call him (sā'il). The owner of the definition who defends the correctness of his definition and demonstrates the invalidity of the objection is called (māni'), and some call him (mudāfi').
B - The objection is directed against the definition regarding violation of its logical conditions. So the objector may object to the definition by one of four matters:
1 - That it is not exhaustive (ghayr muṭṭarid).
2 - That it is not exclusive (ghayr mun'akis).
3 - That it entails an impossibility, such as circular priority, infinite regress, or conjunction of contradictories.
4 - That it is more obscure than the defined term.
As for those who are lenient in verbal definition, like linguists, they do not require it to be comprehensive and exclusive; thus objections against their verbal definitions for being non-comprehensive or non-exclusive are not leveled. It is known that they do not require that in their explanations of vocabulary; rather, they define them in a kind of definition and distinguish them in a kind of distinction from others.
C - And the responses with which the defender (māni'), the owner of the definition, repels the objections directed by the objector against the definition, revert to what follows:
First: If the objection directed against the definition includes that the definition is not comprehensive or not exclusive, i.e., not exhaustive or not coextensive, then the responses revert to what they call 'taḥrīr al-murād' (clarification of intent).
And clarification of intent here is in four ways:
First way: Clarifying the intent regarding the defined term.
Second way: Clarifying the intent regarding some parts of the definition.
Third way: Clarifying the intent regarding the scholarly doctrine upon which the definition is based.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage establishes the procedural rules for debating definitions. It first names the participants: the objector (مستدل or سائل) and the defender (مانع or مدافع). Then it lists four permissible grounds for objection: (1) the definition is not exhaustive (غير مطرد) — i.e., it fails to include all instances of the defined term; (2) it is not exclusive (غير منعكس) — i.e., it includes things that are not instances; (3) it leads to an impossibility like circularity, infinite regress, or contradiction; (4) it is more obscure than the term being defined. A special note is made that linguists, who are lax about verbal definitions, are not subject to the first two objections because they do not require their definitions to be comprehensive and exclusive. Finally, the responses of the defender are summarized under the concept of 'taḥrīr al-murād' (clarification of intent). When the objection is that the definition is not comprehensive or exclusive, the defender clarifies his intent regarding the defined term, parts of the definition, or the underlying scholarly doctrine (a fourth way is implied but not fully listed here). This shows that the validity of a definition can be defended by disambiguating what exactly is meant.

Key Terms
مستدل
مانع
غير مطرد
غير منعكس
تحرير المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rules, which of the following is NOT a valid ground for objecting to a logical definition?
A) The definition is not exhaustive.
B) The definition is not exclusive.
C) The definition is too brief.
D) The definition entails circularity.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, a definition is refuted only by specific logical flaws; mere insufficiency of detail is not a valid objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Practice a debate: Take the definition 'al-ʿibāda is submission with love and humility.' Object using one of the four grounds (e.g., it is not exhaustive: does it include all forms of worship?). Then, as defender, use taḥrīr al-murād to clarify what is meant by 'submission.'
?
Identify the valid objection in a given scenario: A scholar defines 'ijtihad' as 'expending effort to derive a ruling.' Another scholar objects that this definition includes the effort of a student. Is this a valid objection? Which ground? (Answer: non-exclusiveness, ghayr mun'akis.)

Roles and Objections in Dialectical Definition
Author defines the roles of mustadill and mani' and lists four objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of mustadill and mani' and list the four valid objections to a definition in Islamic jadal.

Core Idea
In dialectic, the mustadill objects using four conditions; the mani' defends by clarifying intent.

This passage introduces the two protagonists in definitional jadal: the mustadill (objector) and the mani' (defender). You will learn the four grounds on which a definition can be challenged: not coextensive, not converse, entailing absurdity, or being more obscure. Understanding these roles and objections is essential for participating in or analyzing a dialectical exchange in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
Recognize the mustadill (objector) who challenges the definition, and the mani' (defender) who upholds it. In the passage, the mustadill is also called sa'il, and the mani' is also called mudafi'.
The roles are fixed in the exchange: the mustadill always initiates the objection.
2
Check the first condition: coextensiveness (muttarid)
Determine if the definition covers all instances of the definiendum. If not, it is a valid objection that the definition is not jami' (comprehensive).
This is also called being 'muttarid'.
3
Check the second condition: exclusiveness (mun'akis)
Determine if the definition excludes non-instances. If it includes things that are not the definiendum, it is not mani' (exclusive).
This is also called being 'mun'akis'.
4
Check for absurdities and clarity
The definition must not entail impossible consequences like circularity (dawr), infinite regress (tasalsul), or contradiction (ijtima' al-naqidayn). Additionally, the definition must be clearer than the definiendum.
If the definition is more obscure, it fails its purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستدلّ)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (سائل). وصاحب التعريف الذي يدافع عن صحة تعريفه فيبين سقوط الاعتراض، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (مدافع).
ب - يُوجَّه الاعتراض على التعريف بما يخالف شروطه المنطقية، (فللمستدلّ) أن يعترض على التعريف بواحد من أمور أربعة :
١ - بكونه غير مطرد.
٢ - بكونه غير منعكس.
٣- بكونه يستلزم المحال كالدور السبقي، وكالتسلسل، وكاجتماع النقيضين .
٤ - بكونه أخفى من المعرَّف.
وعند من يتساهل في التعريف اللفظي كاللُّغويين، فلا يرون اشتراط كونه جامعاً مانعاً، لا يُوجَّه الاعتراض على تعريفاتهم اللفظية بأنها غير جامعة أو غير مانعة. فالمعروف أنهم لا يشترطون ذلك في بياناتهم للمفردات، وإنما يُعرِّفونها نوع تعريف، ويُميِّزونها نوع تمييز عن غيرها.
جـ - والأجوبة التي يدفع بها (المانع) صاحب التعريف، الاعتراضات التي يُوجِّهها (المستدل) على التعريف؛ ترجع إلى ما يلي :
أولاً : إذا كان الاعتراض الموجه على التعريف يتضمن كون التعريف غير جامع أو غير مانع، أي : غير مطرد أو غير منعكس، فالأجوبة ترجع إلى ما يطلقون عليه عبارة (تحرير المراد).
ويكون تحرير المراد هنا بأربعة وجوه :
الوجه الأول : تحرير المراد من المعرَّف.
الوجه الثاني : تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف.
الوجه الثالث : تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التعريف .
الوجه الرابع : تحرير المراد من نوع التعريف .
وفيما يلي شرح هذه الوجوه الأربعة :
تحرير المراد من المعرَّف :
قد يختلف التعريف باختلاف المراد من الشيء المعرَّف، فيتوجه اعتراض (المستدلّ) وفق فهمه المخالف لمراد (المانع) صاحب التعريف، وعندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من المعرَّف، ويندفع بذلك الاعتراض .
مثال ذلك : أن يقدم صاحب التعريف تعريفاً للدابة بأنها كل حيوان يمشي على أربع قوائم ، فيعترض (المستدلّ) عليه بأن هذا التعريف غير جامع ، إذ لا يشمل ما يدبُّ على الأرض من غير ذوات الأربع ، فيدفعُ (المانع) صاحب التعريف هذا الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من المعرَّف ، فيقول له : إنني أردت ما يطلق عليه اسم دابَّةٍ عُرْفاً ، ولم أرد ما يطلق عليه اسم دابة لغة .
تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف :
قد يختلف فهم صيغة التعريف، أو فهم بعض عناصر منه، فيفهم المعترض (المستدلّ) منه ما لم يُرِدْه (المانع) صاحب التعريف، وبسبب ذلك يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف، عندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مُراده من الجزء الذي سبَّب فهمُه على خلاف المراد منه اعتراض (المستدلّ) .
مثال ذلك : أن يقدّم صاحب التعريف تعريفاً للحيوان بأنه كل جسم نامٍ حسَّاس مفكر، فيفهم المعترض (المستدل) من لفظة (مفكر) معنى خاصاً بالتفكير الإنساني، فيعترض على التعريف بأنه غير جامع ، لخروج غير الإنسان من المعرف ، فيدفع (المانع) صاحب التعريف هذا الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من لفظة (مفكر) الواردة في تعريفه ، فيقول : أردت من التفكير كل نشاط في المخ ينشأ عنه حركة إرادية، أو كل نشاط في الجسم ينشأ عنه حركة إرادية، وهذا موجود لدى كل الكائنات الحية .
A mustadill (questioner), and some call him sa'il. The owner of the definition who defends the soundness of his definition and shows the fall of the objection is called a mani' (defender), and some call him mubafic.
B. The objection is directed against the definition by what violates its logical conditions. The mustadill can object to the definition by one of four matters:
1. That it is not coextensive (muttarid).
2. That it is not converse (mun'akis).
3. That it entails an impossibility, such as circular precedence, infinite regress, or conjunction of contradictories.
4. That it is more obscure than the definiendum.
As for those who are lenient in lexical definition, such as linguists, who do not require it to be comprehensive and exclusive, no objection is directed against their lexical definitions that they are not comprehensive or not exclusive. It is known that they do not require that in their explanations of singular terms, but rather they define them with a kind of definition and distinguish them with a kind of distinction from others.
C. The answers by which the mani', the owner of the definition, defends against the objections directed by the mustadill against the definition revert to the following:
First: If the objection directed against the definition implies that the definition is not comprehensive or not exclusive, i.e., not coextensive or not converse, then the answers revert to what they call 'tahrir al-murad' (clarification of the intended meaning).
And tahrir al-murad here is in four ways:
The first way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the definiendum.
The second way: Clarification of the intended meaning from some parts of the definition.
The third way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the scientific doctrine upon which the definition is built.
The fourth way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the type of definition.
Below is the explanation of these four ways:
Clarification of the intended meaning from the definiendum:
The definition may differ according to the intended meaning of the thing defined. Then the objection of the mustadill is directed according to his understanding contrary to the intent of the mani', the owner of the definition. In that case, the answer of the mani' is by clarifying his intention from the definiendum, and the objection is repelled.
Example: The owner of the definition gives a definition of 'dabba' (creature) as 'every animal that walks on four legs'. The mustadill objects that this definition is not comprehensive, since it does not include what creeps on the ground without four legs. The mani', the owner of the definition, repels this objection by clarifying his intention from the definiendum, saying: 'I intended what is customarily called dabba, not what is called dabba linguistically.'
Clarification of the intended meaning from some parts of the definition:
The understanding of the formulation of the definition may differ, or the understanding of some elements of it, so the objector (mustadill) understands from it what the mani', the owner of the definition, did not intend. Because of that, he directs his objection against the definition. In that case, the answer of the mani' is by clarifying his intention from the part which, by understanding it contrary to what was intended, caused the objection of the mustadill.
Example: The owner of the definition gives a definition of 'animal' as 'every growing, sensing, thinking body'. The objector (mustadill) understands from the word 'thinking' a specific meaning of human thinking, and objects that the definition is not comprehensive, because non-human things are excluded from the definiendum. The mani', the owner of the definition, repels this objection by clarifying his intention from the word 'thinking' occurring in his definition, saying: 'I intended by thinking every activity in the brain that results in voluntary movement, or every activity in the body that results in voluntary movement, and this exists in all living beings.'
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage describes the dialectical method (jadal) concerning definitions. It first introduces the two key roles: the mustadill (questioner/objector) and the mani' (defender of the definition). The mustadill challenges the definition based on four possible faults: (1) being not coextensive (muttarid) — failing to cover all instances; (2) being not converse (mun'akis) — including non-instances; (3) entailing logical absurdities like circularity, infinite regress, or contradiction; (4) being more obscure than the definiendum. The passage notes that lexicographers (lughawiyyun) often do not require definitions to be comprehensive and exclusive, so such objections do not apply to their lexical definitions. The core of the passage then presents the defender's responses, all falling under 'tahrir al-murad' (clarifying the intended meaning). There are four ways to do this: (1) clarifying the intended meaning of the definiendum itself — e.g., defining 'dabba' (creature) as 'an animal that walks on four legs' may be criticized as not covering crawling animals, but the defender clarifies he means the customary definition, not the linguistic one. (2) clarifying the intended meaning of a part of the definition — e.g., defining 'animal' as 'growing, sensing, thinking body' may be objected that 'thinking' excludes non-humans, but the defender clarifies that he means any brain activity causing voluntary movement, present in all living beings. (3) clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine on which the definition is based; (4) clarifying the intended meaning of the type of definition (e.g., logical vs. lexical). The passage thus provides a structured method for resolving definitional disputes in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
مستدلّ
مانع
مطرد
منعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four valid objections to a definition?
A) That it is not coextensive (muttarid)
B) That it is not converse (mun'akis)
C) That it is too broad
D) That it entails an impossibility

Key Takeaway
The four objections (non-muttarid, non-mun'akis, leading to absurdity, obscurer than definiendum) provide a systematic way to challenge any definition in Islamic dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a dialectical exchange, you are the mustadill. The mani' defines 'ijma'' as 'the agreement of the mujtahids of the umma after the Prophet's death on a sharia ruling'. Apply the four objections: is it coextensive? Is it exclusive? Does it entail absurdity? Is it clearer than the definiendum? Write a brief objection using one of the four grounds.

intermediate
10 min
Responding with Tahrir al-Murad
Author details four responses via tahrir al-murad with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four methods of tahrir al-murad to defend a definition against objections in dialectical argumentation.

Core Idea
The mani' can rebut objections by clarifying his intended meaning of the definiendum, parts of the definition, underlying doctrine, or definition type.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage: how the defender (mani') can answer objections using 'tahrir al-murad' (clarifying the intended meaning). Four distinct ways are presented, each illustrated with examples. Mastering these responses is crucial for effectively defending a definition in a jadal setting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Determine whether the objection is about comprehensiveness (not muttarid) or exclusiveness (not mun'akis). If it is about these, tahrir al-murad is the appropriate response.
Tahrir al-murad is used specifically for objections concerning jami' and mani'.
2
Apply the first way: clarify the definiendum
Show that the objector misunderstands what the definiendum refers to. For example, 'dabba' in custom differs from 'dabba' in language.
This is tahrir al-murad min al-mu'arraf.
3
Apply the second way: clarify a part of the definition
Explain that a term in the definition is being misinterpreted. For example, 'thinking' in the definition of animal need not be human-specific.
This is tahrir al-murad min ba'd ajza' al-ta'rif.
4
Apply the third or fourth way: clarify the doctrine or definition type
Clarify the scientific school (madhhab) on which the definition is built, or specify whether it is a logical definition (hadd) or a lexical definition (ta'rif lafzi).
These ways handle deeper disagreements about assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستدلّ)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (سائل). وصاحب التعريف الذي يدافع عن صحة تعريفه فيبين سقوط الاعتراض، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع)، وبعضهم يطلق عليه اسم (مدافع).
ب - يُوجَّه الاعتراض على التعريف بما يخالف شروطه المنطقية، (فللمستدلّ) أن يعترض على التعريف بواحد من أمور أربعة :
١ - بكونه غير مطرد.
٢ - بكونه غير منعكس.
٣- بكونه يستلزم المحال كالدور السبقي، وكالتسلسل، وكاجتماع النقيضين .
٤ - بكونه أخفى من المعرَّف.
وعند من يتساهل في التعريف اللفظي كاللُّغويين، فلا يرون اشتراط كونه جامعاً مانعاً، لا يُوجَّه الاعتراض على تعريفاتهم اللفظية بأنها غير جامعة أو غير مانعة. فالمعروف أنهم لا يشترطون ذلك في بياناتهم للمفردات، وإنما يُعرِّفونها نوع تعريف، ويُميِّزونها نوع تمييز عن غيرها.
جـ - والأجوبة التي يدفع بها (المانع) صاحب التعريف، الاعتراضات التي يُوجِّهها (المستدل) على التعريف؛ ترجع إلى ما يلي :
أولاً : إذا كان الاعتراض الموجه على التعريف يتضمن كون التعريف غير جامع أو غير مانع، أي : غير مطرد أو غير منعكس، فالأجوبة ترجع إلى ما يطلقون عليه عبارة (تحرير المراد).
ويكون تحرير المراد هنا بأربعة وجوه :
الوجه الأول : تحرير المراد من المعرَّف.
الوجه الثاني : تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف.
الوجه الثالث : تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التعريف .
الوجه الرابع : تحرير المراد من نوع التعريف .
وفيما يلي شرح هذه الوجوه الأربعة :
تحرير المراد من المعرَّف :
قد يختلف التعريف باختلاف المراد من الشيء المعرَّف، فيتوجه اعتراض (المستدلّ) وفق فهمه المخالف لمراد (المانع) صاحب التعريف، وعندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من المعرَّف، ويندفع بذلك الاعتراض .
مثال ذلك : أن يقدم صاحب التعريف تعريفاً للدابة بأنها كل حيوان يمشي على أربع قوائم ، فيعترض (المستدلّ) عليه بأن هذا التعريف غير جامع ، إذ لا يشمل ما يدبُّ على الأرض من غير ذوات الأربع ، فيدفعُ (المانع) صاحب التعريف هذا الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من المعرَّف ، فيقول له : إنني أردت ما يطلق عليه اسم دابَّةٍ عُرْفاً ، ولم أرد ما يطلق عليه اسم دابة لغة .
تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف :
قد يختلف فهم صيغة التعريف، أو فهم بعض عناصر منه، فيفهم المعترض (المستدلّ) منه ما لم يُرِدْه (المانع) صاحب التعريف، وبسبب ذلك يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف، عندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مُراده من الجزء الذي سبَّب فهمُه على خلاف المراد منه اعتراض (المستدلّ) .
مثال ذلك : أن يقدّم صاحب التعريف تعريفاً للحيوان بأنه كل جسم نامٍ حسَّاس مفكر، فيفهم المعترض (المستدل) من لفظة (مفكر) معنى خاصاً بالتفكير الإنساني، فيعترض على التعريف بأنه غير جامع ، لخروج غير الإنسان من المعرف ، فيدفع (المانع) صاحب التعريف هذا الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من لفظة (مفكر) الواردة في تعريفه ، فيقول : أردت من التفكير كل نشاط في المخ ينشأ عنه حركة إرادية، أو كل نشاط في الجسم ينشأ عنه حركة إرادية، وهذا موجود لدى كل الكائنات الحية .
A mustadill (questioner), and some call him sa'il. The owner of the definition who defends the soundness of his definition and shows the fall of the objection is called a mani' (defender), and some call him mubafic.
B. The objection is directed against the definition by what violates its logical conditions. The mustadill can object to the definition by one of four matters:
1. That it is not coextensive (muttarid).
2. That it is not converse (mun'akis).
3. That it entails an impossibility, such as circular precedence, infinite regress, or conjunction of contradictories.
4. That it is more obscure than the definiendum.
As for those who are lenient in lexical definition, such as linguists, who do not require it to be comprehensive and exclusive, no objection is directed against their lexical definitions that they are not comprehensive or not exclusive. It is known that they do not require that in their explanations of singular terms, but rather they define them with a kind of definition and distinguish them with a kind of distinction from others.
C. The answers by which the mani', the owner of the definition, defends against the objections directed by the mustadill against the definition revert to the following:
First: If the objection directed against the definition implies that the definition is not comprehensive or not exclusive, i.e., not coextensive or not converse, then the answers revert to what they call 'tahrir al-murad' (clarification of the intended meaning).
And tahrir al-murad here is in four ways:
The first way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the definiendum.
The second way: Clarification of the intended meaning from some parts of the definition.
The third way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the scientific doctrine upon which the definition is built.
The fourth way: Clarification of the intended meaning from the type of definition.
Below is the explanation of these four ways:
Clarification of the intended meaning from the definiendum:
The definition may differ according to the intended meaning of the thing defined. Then the objection of the mustadill is directed according to his understanding contrary to the intent of the mani', the owner of the definition. In that case, the answer of the mani' is by clarifying his intention from the definiendum, and the objection is repelled.
Example: The owner of the definition gives a definition of 'dabba' (creature) as 'every animal that walks on four legs'. The mustadill objects that this definition is not comprehensive, since it does not include what creeps on the ground without four legs. The mani', the owner of the definition, repels this objection by clarifying his intention from the definiendum, saying: 'I intended what is customarily called dabba, not what is called dabba linguistically.'
Clarification of the intended meaning from some parts of the definition:
The understanding of the formulation of the definition may differ, or the understanding of some elements of it, so the objector (mustadill) understands from it what the mani', the owner of the definition, did not intend. Because of that, he directs his objection against the definition. In that case, the answer of the mani' is by clarifying his intention from the part which, by understanding it contrary to what was intended, caused the objection of the mustadill.
Example: The owner of the definition gives a definition of 'animal' as 'every growing, sensing, thinking body'. The objector (mustadill) understands from the word 'thinking' a specific meaning of human thinking, and objects that the definition is not comprehensive, because non-human things are excluded from the definiendum. The mani', the owner of the definition, repels this objection by clarifying his intention from the word 'thinking' occurring in his definition, saying: 'I intended by thinking every activity in the brain that results in voluntary movement, or every activity in the body that results in voluntary movement, and this exists in all living beings.'
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage describes the dialectical method (jadal) concerning definitions. It first introduces the two key roles: the mustadill (questioner/objector) and the mani' (defender of the definition). The mustadill challenges the definition based on four possible faults: (1) being not coextensive (muttarid) — failing to cover all instances; (2) being not converse (mun'akis) — including non-instances; (3) entailing logical absurdities like circularity, infinite regress, or contradiction; (4) being more obscure than the definiendum. The passage notes that lexicographers (lughawiyyun) often do not require definitions to be comprehensive and exclusive, so such objections do not apply to their lexical definitions. The core of the passage then presents the defender's responses, all falling under 'tahrir al-murad' (clarifying the intended meaning). There are four ways to do this: (1) clarifying the intended meaning of the definiendum itself — e.g., defining 'dabba' (creature) as 'an animal that walks on four legs' may be criticized as not covering crawling animals, but the defender clarifies he means the customary definition, not the linguistic one. (2) clarifying the intended meaning of a part of the definition — e.g., defining 'animal' as 'growing, sensing, thinking body' may be objected that 'thinking' excludes non-humans, but the defender clarifies that he means any brain activity causing voluntary movement, present in all living beings. (3) clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine on which the definition is based; (4) clarifying the intended meaning of the type of definition (e.g., logical vs. lexical). The passage thus provides a structured method for resolving definitional disputes in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
تحرير المراد
مطرد
منعكس
مستدلّ
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of defining 'dabba' as 'every animal that walks on four legs', what does the defender (mani') clarify to rebut the objection that the definition is not comprehensive?
A) He clarifies that 'dabba' in language excludes creeping animals.
B) He clarifies that he meant 'dabba' in the customary sense, not the linguistic sense.
C) He clarifies that 'dabba' must be defined by its differentia 'walks on four legs'.
D) He clarifies that the definiendum is 'dabba' as a generic term.

Key Takeaway
Tahrir al-murad allows the defender to resolve objections by clarifying the intended meaning of the definiendum, parts of the definition, underlying doctrine, or definition type, without changing the definition itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
As the mani', you defined 'ijma'' as 'the agreement of the mujtahids of the umma after the Prophet's death on a sharia ruling'. The mustadill objects that it excludes rulings agreed upon during the Prophet's life. Use tahrir al-murad min al-mu'arraf to respond.
?
In a second exchange, the mustadill objects to your definition of 'qiyas' as 'applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared 'illah'. He says it is not comprehensive because it excludes qiyas al-dalala (indicational analogy). Use tahrir al-murad min ba'd ajza' al-ta'rif by clarifying what you mean by ''illah'.

Deflecting Objections by Clarifying the Scientific School
Author explains how to answer objections by clarifying the scientific school underlying a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scientific school underlying a definition and use it to defend the definition against objections based on different premises.

Core Idea
A definition is valid within its own school; clarifying the school refutes cross-school objections.

In Islamic dialectics, definitions are often built on premises specific to a school (madhhab). When an objector attacks a definition using premises from another school, the defender's most effective response is to state his school explicitly. This move neutralizes the objection without changing the definition. This lesson teaches you how to apply this tool in real debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying school
Look at the definition and determine which scientific or doctrinal school it relies on. For example, the definition of 'thing' as 'every known existent' presupposes the theological school that denies existence to non-existents.
2
Recognise the objector's school
The objector often uses premises from a different school. Note that the objector's complaint that the definition is not comprehensive (in the 'thing' example) stems from the philosophical school that considers non-existent things as things.
3
Formulate the defender's response
The defender simply clarifies: 'I built my definition on school X, not on school Y.' This makes the objection irrelevant because the definition was never intended to meet the objector's criteria.
No need to alter the definition; only clarify the intended madhhab.
4
Conclude the objection is deflected
Once the school is specified, the objection is seen as based on premises that do not apply. The definition remains valid within its own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التعريف: قد يختلف التعريف باختلاف المذهب العلمي الذي بُني عليه، فيقدّم صاحب التعريف تعريفه وفق المذهب الذي يذهب إليه، ويعترض عليه (المستدلّ) وفق مذهب آخر يذهب هو إليه، عندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) صاحب التعريف ببيان المذهب الذي بنى عليه تعريفه، ويندفع بذلك الاعتراض على التعريف. مثال ذلك: أن يقدم صاحب التعريف تعريفاً للفظ (الشيء) بأنه (كل معلوم موجود) وفق مذهب علماء الكلام من أهل السنة، فيعترض (المستدل) عليه بأن هذا التعريف غير جامع، لأنه لا يشمل غير الموجود من المعلومات، ويبني اعتراضه على مذهب الفلاسفة الذين يطلقون اسم (الشيء) على المعلوم ولو لم يكن موجوداً بالفعل، فيدفع (المانع) صاحب التعريف هذا الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه تعريفه، فيقول إنما بنيت تعريفي على مذهب علماء الكلام من أهل السنة. مثال آخر: تعريف الفقيه الوضوء والغسل بأنهما: طهارة خاصة مَنْوِيَّة بماء طهور، فيعترض عليه فقيه آخر بأن هذا التعريف غير جامع، لأنه لا يشمل الطهارة غير المَنْوِيَّة، فيجيب صاحب التعريف بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه تعريفه فيقول: إنما بنيت تعريفي على مذهب من يشترط النية في طهارتي الوضوء والغسل، كالإمام الشافعي مثلاً. ومن تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي، بيان أن التعريف إنما كان تعريفاً لفظياً على مذهب من لا يشترط في التعريف اللفظي أن يكون جامعاً مانعاً، وإنما يكفي فيه تمييز المعرَّف بأي وجه من وجوه التميز، كما يفعل اللغويون في تعريف المفردات اللغوية.
Clarifying the intended scientific school upon which the definition is built: The definition may differ according to the scientific school upon which it is built. So the proponent of the definition presents his definition according to the school he follows, and the objector objects to it according to another school that he follows. At that point, the response of the defender, the proponent of the definition, is to state the school upon which he built his definition, and the objection to the definition is thereby repelled. Example of that: The proponent of the definition presents a definition of the word 'thing' as 'every known existent' according to the school of the theologians of the Ahl al-Sunnah. The objector objects to it that this definition is not comprehensive because it does not include non-existent known things, and he builds his objection on the school of the philosophers who apply the name 'thing' to the known even if it is not actually existent. The defender, the proponent of the definition, repels this objection by clarifying his intended scientific school upon which he built his definition, saying: 'I only built my definition on the school of the theologians of the Ahl al-Sunnah.' Another example: The jurist's definition of wudu and ghusl as 'a specific intended purification with pure water'. Another jurist objects to him that this definition is not comprehensive because it does not include unintended purification. The proponent of the definition answers by clarifying his intended scientific school upon which he built his definition, saying: 'I only built my definition on the school of those who require intention in the purifications of wudu and ghusl, like Imam al-Shafi'i for instance.' And from the clarification of the intended scientific school is the explanation that the definition is merely a verbal definition according to the school of those who do not require in verbal definition that it be comprehensive and exclusive; rather, it suffices that it distinguishes the definiendum in any manner of distinction, as the lexicographers do in defining lexical items.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction on how to respond when an objection to a definition is based on a different scientific school (madhhab). The author explains that definitions often depend on the premises of a particular school. When an objector (al-mustadill) raises an objection using premises from another school, the defender (al-mani') simply clarifies his own school, thereby deflecting the objection. Two examples are given: first, the definition of 'thing' as 'every known existent' according to the theologians (mutakallimun) versus the philosophers who consider non-existent knowable things as things; second, the definition of wudu and ghusl as 'a specific intended purification' according to those who require intention (niyya), like the Shafi'is, versus those who do not. Additionally, the author notes that sometimes the definition is merely lexical (lafzi) meaning it only needs to distinguish, not be comprehensive and exclusive, as lexicographers do. The key logical point is that objections based on differing foundational premises are invalid if the defender specifies his own school.

Key Terms
تحرير المراد
المذهب العلمي
المستدل
المانع

D) By providing a longer definition.

Key Takeaway
Clarifying the intended school is a powerful dialectical move that makes objections based on different premises irrelevant.

Using Definition Types to Deflect Objections
Author explains how to answer objections by specifying the kind of definition given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a debater can use the distinction between hadd, rasm, and verbal definition to answer an objection against his definition.

Core Idea
A definition's success depends on its intended type, not on being a perfect hadd.

In a debate about definitions, a proponent may be attacked for giving an incomplete or non-essential definition. The author of this passage teaches a simple defensive move: clarify that the definition was not meant to be a complete hadd, but rather a rasm, a deficient hadd, or a verbal synonym. This shifts the ground of debate and repels the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim
Determine what definition the proponent has given. Note whether it is a proper definition (hadd), a description (rasm), or a verbal synonym.
2
Identify the objector's complaint
The objector points out that the definition fails as a perfect hadd. What specific deficiency does he claim?
The objector might say it is not complete, or not essential.
3
Identify the defender's response
The defender clarifies the intended type: incomplete hadd, rasm, incomplete rasm, or verbal definition. This shows the objection is based on a mistaken assumption.
For example: 'I only meant it as a rasm, not a hadd.'
4
Evaluate the response
Determine whether the clarification truly defeats the objection. If the objector's complaint is irrelevant to the intended type, the response is effective.
The passage says 'thereby the objection is repelled.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحرير المراد من نوع التعريف: قد يُقدِّم صاحب التعريف تعريفه على أنه رسم وليس بحدّ، أو أنه ناقص وليس بتامّ، أو أنه تعريف لفظي بالمرادف، فيوجّه (المستدلّ) اعتراضه على أن هذا التعريف لا يصلح (حداً) أو ليس بتام، فيجيب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من نوع التعريف، فيقول مثلاً : إنما أردتُه (حداً ناقصاً)، أو (رسماً)، أو (رسماً ناقصاً)، أو (تعريفاً لفظياً بالمرادف)، أو نحو هذا، وبذلك يندفع الاعتراض. ويسهل تصور الأمثلة، وفي بحوث التعريفات أمثلة كثيرة يمكن تصوير مناظرة على أساسها .
Clarifying the intended type of definition: The proponent of the definition may present his definition as a description (rasm) and not a proper definition (hadd), or as incomplete (naqis) and not complete (tamm), or as a verbal definition by synonym (lafzi bi al-muradif). The objector directs his objection that this definition is not suitable as a proper definition (hadd) or is not complete. The defender answers by clarifying his intended type of definition, saying for example: I only meant it as an incomplete proper definition (hadd naqis), or a description (rasm), or an incomplete description (rasm naqis), or a verbal definition by synonym, or similar to that. And thereby the objection is repelled. It is easy to imagine examples, and in discussions of definitions there are many examples upon which a debate can be modeled.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of clarifying intent, now focusing on the type of definition (naw' al-ta'rif). The author explains that a proponent might intend his definition to be a rasm (description) rather than a hadd (proper definition), or incomplete rather than complete, or a verbal definition by synonym. The objector might attack it as if it were meant to be a perfect hadd. The defender simply clarifies that he intended a lesser type, and the objection fails. For instance, a definition that merely describes the definiendum by its properties (rasm) does not need to give the essence. The passage encourages the reader to imagine examples, noting that many exist in discussions of definitions.

B) A complete description (rasm tamm).
C) An incomplete proper definition (hadd naqis).
D) A definition by cause.

Key Takeaway
Clarifying the intended type of definition (hadd, rasm, verbal) is a key dialectical move to repel objections that misapply the standard of a perfect hadd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defending a Definition: The Relativity of Manifestness
Author explains that manifestness is relative to the audience, so an objection fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that a definition is not more manifest by appealing to audience-relative knowledge.

Core Idea
Manifestness is relative to the audience, not absolute.

In dialectic, one common objection to a definition is that it is not more manifest (azhar) than the definiendum. But as the author explains, manifestness is not an absolute property—it depends on the knowledge level of the audience. This lesson explores how a defender can rebut such an objection by showing that the definiens was chosen precisely because it is more manifest for the intended addressee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The critic claims the definiens is not clearer than the definiendum. Pinpoint the exact basis of the objection: it assumes manifestness is an absolute property.
2
Understand the Principle
Recognize that zuhur and khafa' are relative; they vary with the knowledge and context of the audience. This is the key logical move.
The author states 'الظهور والخفاء نسبيان' (manifestness and hiddenness are relative).
3
Craft the Response
Deny the objection by specifying the intended audience. The defender says: 'I intended to address people of this country, so I brought them what is more manifest to them.'
The response is a simple denial (man') based on audience specificity.
4
Apply the Example
The author gives the example of defining 'hinta' (wheat) as 'burr' (barley) in a region where barley is better known. This illustrates the relativity in action.
In another region, the definition could be reversed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : وإذا كان الاعتراض الموجه على التعريف يتضمن كون التعريف غير أظهر من المعرَّف، أمكن الجواب بمنع ذلك، باعتبار أن الظهور والخفاء نسبيان، يُراعى فيهما دائماً حالُ المخاطب، فقد نُعرِّفُ في بلدٍ الحنطة بأنها البر، لأن البر لدى أهل هذا البلد أظهر من الحنطة، وقد نعكس الأمر في بلد آخر، لأن الحنطة لدى أهله أظهر من البر. ويقول صاحب التعريف : إنما أردت مخاطبة أهل هذا البلد، فجئت لهم بالأظهر، فعرَّفْتُ لهم به الأخفى .
Secondly: If the objection directed to the definition includes that the definition is not more manifest than the definiendum, it is possible to answer by denying that, considering that manifestness and hiddenness are relative; the situation of the addressee is always taken into account in them. For we may define in a country 'wheat' (hinta) as 'barley' (burr) because barley is more manifest to the people of that country than wheat, and we might reverse the matter in another country because wheat is more manifest to its people than barley. The proponent of the definition says: I only intended to address the people of this country, so I brought them what is more manifest, and defined for them the more hidden with it.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage addresses a common objection that a definition is not more manifest (azhar) than the definiendum. The author states that manifestness (zuhur) and hiddenness (khafa') are relative — they depend on the audience. Therefore, the defender can simply deny the objection by pointing out that for his intended audience, the definiens is indeed more manifest. The example of defining 'wheat' (hinta) as 'barley' (burr) in a region where barley is better known illustrates the relativity. The key logical principle is that definitions must be from the known to the unknown relative to the learner; absolute manifestness is not required.

C) Because wheat is a type of barley.

Key Takeaway
A definition can be defended against the 'not more manifest' objection by pointing out that manifestness is relative to the audience.

Defending a Definition from Circularity: Dawr Ma'i vs Dawr Sabqi
Author shows how to answer the objection of circularity by distinguishing types of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between coincidental and sequential circularity and apply the aspect distinction to defend a definition against circularity objections.

Core Idea
Not all circularity is vicious; coincidental circularity with distinct aspects saves a definition.

In dialectical debates over definitions, one common objection is that the definition involves circularity, which is considered impossible. The defender can rebut this by showing that the circularity is only in appearance, not in reality. This lesson teaches you how to recognize the two types of circularity and how to break the circle by distinguishing the aspects (jiha) of the terms involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity objection
Find the two terms in the definition that seem to depend on each other. For example, in 'knowledge is apprehension of the known', 'knowledge' and 'known' appear interdependent.
2
Determine the type of circularity
Ask: is the dependence in the same respect (sequential, vicious) or in different respects (coincidental, permissible)? Sequential circularity (dawr sabqi) makes a definition impossible; coincidental (dawr ma'i) does not.
The passage states that al-dawr al-ma'i is permissible.
3
Locate the aspect distinction
Look for a difference in the domain, usage, or perspective of the terms. In the example, 'known' is used in common parlance (urf) to mean any object with a mental image, while 'knowledge' is being defined philosophically.
This breaks the circle because the meanings are not identical.
4
Apply the distinction to the specific definition
Show that the circularity is not vicious because the two terms are understood in different aspects. The defender says the jiha is munfakka (distinct), so the circle is not exact.
5
Construct a valid defense
Formulate a response: admit there is a verbal circle, but argue it is coincidental due to distinct aspects, thus the definition does not entail impossibility.
This is the standard dialectical move in such objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً : وإذا كان الاعتراض الموجه على التعريف يتضمن كون التعريف يستلزم المحال، كالدور مثلاً، أمكن الجواب بمنع هذا الاستلزام، وذلك ببيان أن الدور مثلاً من قبيل (الدور المَعِيّ) وهو جائز لا يلزم عنه المحال، وليس من قبيل (الدور السبقي)، وببيان أن الجهة منفكة فالدور غير متطابق لانفكاك الجهة . مثال ذلك : تعريف العلم بأنه : إدراك المعلوم . فيعترض (المستدلّ) بأن في هذا التعريف (دوراً) لتوقف معرفة العلم على معرفة المعلوم الواردة في التعريف، ولا يعرف كون الشيء معلوماً حتى يُعرَف العلم لأنه مشتق منه، فثبت الدور المحال، فيجيب (المانع) صاحب التعريف بأن الجهة منفكة، وذلك لأن لفظة (المعلوم) تطلق في العرف على كل ما له صورة ثابتة في الذهن، فهي في العرف منفكة عن (العلم) الذي نريد تحديد حقيقته فلسفياً.
Thirdly: If the objection directed to the definition includes that the definition entails impossibility, like circularity for example, it is possible to answer by denying this entailment, by clarifying that the circularity is of the type coincidental circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i), which is permissible and does not entail impossibility, and not of the type sequential circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi), and by clarifying that the aspect (jiha) is distinct, so the circularity is not exact due to the distinctness of the aspect. Example of that: The definition of knowledge as 'apprehension of the known object' (idrak al-ma'lum). The objector objects that in this definition there is circularity because knowledge of knowledge depends on knowledge of the 'known' in the definition, and a thing is not known as known until knowledge is known because it is derived from it, so the impossible circularity is established. The defender, the proponent of the definition, answers that the aspect is distinct, because the word 'known' (al-ma'lum) is applied in common usage to everything that has a fixed mental image; so in common usage it is distinct from 'knowledge' (al-'ilm) whose reality we want to determine philosophically.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage handles objections that a definition involves circularity (dawr), which is considered impossible (muhal). The author explains that the defender can deny the charge by specifying that the circularity is of the 'coincidental' type (al-dawr al-ma'i), which is permissible, not the 'sequential' type (al-dawr al-sabqi) that is impossible. The example is the definition of 'knowledge' ('ilm) as 'apprehension of the known object'. The objector argues that this is circular because knowing 'knowledge' requires knowing what 'known' means, and 'known' derives from 'knowledge'. The defender responds that the two terms are used in different aspects (jiha): 'known' in common usage (urf) refers to any object with a mental image, independent of the philosophical definition of 'knowledge'. Thus the circle is broken. The key logical move is distinguishing the domain of discourse (urf vs. philosophical definition), which shows that the definition does not entail a vicious circle.

Key Terms
الدور المَعِيّ
الدور السبقي
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sequential circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi)
B) Coincidental circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i)
C) Both sequential and coincidental
D) No circularity at all

Key Takeaway
Circularity is a valid objection only if the dependence is in the same aspect; coincidental circularity with distinct aspects is permissible and does not invalidate a definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

An objector claims circularity because 'premises' and 'conclusion' presuppose 'argument'. Show how to defend the definition by distinguishing aspects, using the same method from this lesson.

Implicit Claims and the Man' Response
Author introduces implicit claims and the man' response in definitional debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify implicit claims in a definition and apply the man' response in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Every definition carries hidden claims; spotting them is the key to effective objection.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of implicit claims (daʿāwā ḍimniyya) within definitions. The author explains that when someone provides a definition, they implicitly assert several things about it — that it is complete, essential, or the only correct one. The objector's first task is to recognize these hidden claims and challenge them. The definition provider may respond simply by denying (manʿ) the evidence, without providing counter-evidence. This sets the stage for the structured procedure that follows.

Guided Path
1
Recognize implicit claims
Given a definition, list the claims it implicitly makes beyond the obvious. For example, if someone says 'This is a complete definition', the implicit claim is that it includes all essential parts.
Implicit claims often relate to completeness, essential composition, or exclusivity.
2
Formulate an objection targeting an implicit claim
Choose one implicit claim and state an objection. For instance, object that the definition is not complete because it lacks a necessary differentia.
3
Apply the man' response as provider
As the definition provider, respond by simply denying the objector's evidence without disproving it. You may say 'I deny that your evidence shows incompleteness'.
The man' response is a standard move in jadal; it forces the objector to provide further evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعاوى تتضمن نقض الدعاوى الضمنية التي اشتمل عليها التعريف. ولصاحب التعريف أن يجيب بالمنع على دليل (المستدل)، سواء أذكر دليل المنع أو لم يذكره. فإذا قال صاحب التعريف مثلاً: تعريفي هذا حد تام للمعرَّف، أو حد ناقص له، أو قال: حدُّ الشيء الفلاني كذا، أو رسمه كذا، أو نحو ذلك، فقد اشتمل كلامه على دعوى أو دعاوى ضمنية، بأن تعريفه مؤلف من الذَّاتِيَّات، أو مؤلف من الجنس والفصل القريبين، أو ليس للشيء المعرَّف حدٌّ حقيقي تام غير هذا الحدّ، أو هذا رسم حقيقي، أو نحو ذلك. فالاعتراض عليه حينئذٍ يكون بإبطال هذه الدعاوى الضمنية، مع إقامة الدليل على إبطالها، بإثبات الدعاوى المناقضة لها. ترتيب المناظرة في التعريف تتَّبع الخطوات التالية لدى المناظرة في التعريف: أولاً: ينظر في التعريف، فإذا كان مُورده ناقلاً له عن غيره، غير ملتزم لصحته، فليس للمعترض (المستدل) إلا أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل، ومتى أثبت مُورِدُ التعريف ما يفيد أن نقله صحيح، وفق طرائق إثبات النقول والمرويات، فقد أدى ما عليه وينتهي الأمر بذلك. أما إذا جاء بالتعريف من عنده، أو ذكره دون أن ينقله عن غيره، أو كان ناقلاً له وأعلن التزامه بصحته، فإن مناقشته والاعتراض عليه تكون وفق ما يلي: ثانياً: يُنظر في التعريف، هل فيه لفظ غامض أو موهم لمعنى غير صحيح أو لا؟ فإن كان فيه شيء من ذلك كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يستفسر، أي: أن يطلب تفسير الغامض، أو بيان معنى اللفظ الذي يوهم ما لا يصح، وعلى مُورِد التعريف أن يبين مراده. وإذا لم يكن فيه شيء من ذلك، فإلى الخطوة التالية. ثالثاً: يُنظر في التعريف، هل هو مستوف شروط صحته أو لا؟ فإن كان غير مستوف لذلك، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف بموجب الخلل الذي فيه، بأنه غير جامع، أو غير مانع، أو يستلزم المحال، أو هو أخفى من المعرَّف. ويجيب صاحب التعريف بالأجوبة التي سبق بيانها. وإن كان مستوفياً لشروط صحته، فلا اعتراض عليه من هذا الوجه، وعندئذٍ تأتي الخطوة التالية. رابعاً: ينظر في التعريف، هل تضمن دعاوى ضمنية، بأنه مثلاً حقيقي أو حدّ تام، أو رسم تام، أو نحو ذلك، فإن تضمن شيئاً من ذلك، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه بادّعاء أن التعريف ليس بحقيقي، أو ليس بحد تام، أو ليس برسم تام، وعليه أن يقيم الدليل على ذلك، بما ينتج دعواه، وتجري المناظرة على أساس دعوى المعترض (المستدل)، وفق خطوات المناظرة في التصديقات. خامساً: ثم ينظر في التعريف، هل هو مستوف لشروط حسنة أو لا؟ فإذا كان مستوفياً لشروط حسنة، فلا مجال للاعتراض عليه من هذا الوجه، وإن كان غير مستوف لشروط حسنة، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف، بموجب ما رأى فيه من إخلال بشروط حسنة، ويجيب صاحب التعريف (المانع) بالأجوبة التي سبق بيانها.
Claims include refuting the implicit claims contained in the definition. The definition provider may respond by denying (al-man') the evidence of the arguer (al-mustadill), whether he mentions the evidence for denial or not. So if the definition provider says, for example: 'This definition of mine is a complete definition (hadd tāmm) of the defined, or an incomplete definition (hadd nāqiṣ) of it', or he says: 'The definition of such-and-such thing is such-and-such, or its description (rasm) is such-and-such', or similar, then his speech contains a claim or implicit claims, such that his definition is composed of essential predicates (al-dhātiyyāt), or composed of the proximate genus and differentia, or that the defined thing has no other true complete definition besides this one, or that this is a true description, or similar. So the objection against him then is to invalidate these implicit claims, along with providing evidence for their invalidation, by proving the contradictory claims. The order of disputation in definition follows the following steps in disputation over a definition: First: The definition is examined. If its provider is transmitting it from someone else, not committed to its correctness, then the objector (arguer) may only demand proof of the correctness of the transmission. And whenever the definition provider proves that his transmission is correct, according to the methods of verifying transmissions and narrations, he has fulfilled his duty and the matter ends there. However, if he brings the definition from himself, or mentions it without transmitting it from someone else, or if he transmits it but declares his commitment to its correctness, then its discussion and objection are according to what follows: Second: The definition is examined: does it contain an ambiguous word or one that suggests an incorrect meaning or not? If it contains any of that, the objector (arguer) may inquire, i.e., request an explanation of the ambiguous part, or clarification of the meaning of the word that suggests what is not correct. And the definition provider must clarify his intended meaning. If it does not contain any such thing, then to the next step. Third: The definition is examined: does it fulfill the conditions of its correctness or not? If it does not fulfill them, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection against the definition based on the defect it contains, such as being not comprehensive (ghayr jāmi'), or not exclusive (ghayr māni'), or entailing impossibility (muḥāl), or being more obscure than the defined. And the definition provider responds with the answers that have been previously explained. If it fulfills the conditions of its correctness, then there is no objection from this aspect, and then the next step comes. Fourth: The definition is examined: does it contain implicit claims, such as that it is a real (ḥaqīqī) definition, or a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm), or a complete description (rasm tāmm), or the like? If it contains any of that, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection by claiming that the definition is not real, or not a complete definition, or not a complete description, and he must provide evidence for that, which yields his claim. And the disputation proceeds on the basis of the claim of the objector (arguer), according to the steps of disputation in assents (taṣdīqāt). Fifth: Then the definition is examined: does it fulfill the conditions of a good definition (shurūṭ ḥusn) or not? If it fulfills the conditions of goodness, then there is no room for objection from this aspect. If it does not fulfill the conditions of goodness, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection against the definition based on what he sees as a violation of the conditions of goodness, and the definition provider (al-māni') responds with the answers that have been previously explained.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the procedural order for disputing definitions in dialectical debate (jadal). It begins by explaining that definitions often carry implicit claims (daʿāwā ḍimniyya), such as the claim that the definition is composed of essential attributes or includes the proximate genus and differentia. The objector's role is to refute these implicit claims with evidence, while the definition provider may simply deny (manʿ) the evidence without necessarily providing counterevidence. The heart of the passage is a five-step sequence for examining a definition. Step one checks whether the definition is transmitted from an authority or the provider's own. If transmitted without commitment to its correctness, only the transmission's authenticity needs verification; otherwise, the full procedure applies. Step two checks for ambiguous or misleading wording; the objector may request clarification. Step three tests the definition against standard validity conditions: it must be comprehensive (jāmiʿ), exclusive (māniʿ), not entail impossibility, and not be more obscure than the defined. Step four examines implicit claims like being a 'true definition' or 'complete definition'; the objector must provide proof that the definition falls short. Step five evaluates the definition's aesthetic qualities (shurūṭ ḥusn), such as clarity, conciseness, and avoidance of superfluous terms. The passage is a practical guide for students learning the art of dialectic, showing how to systematically challenge a definition without overlooking any aspect.

دعوى ضمنية
منع

D) It is comprehensive and exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Implicit claims in definitions are the real targets of objection; the man' response allows the provider to deny evidence without disproving it.

Initial Checks: Transmission, Ambiguity, and Validity Conditions
Author details steps one to three of the disputation procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first three steps of the disputation procedure: verifying transmission, clarifying ambiguous terms, and checking validity conditions.

Core Idea
Before challenging content, check transmission and clarity; then test validity conditions systematically.

This lesson covers the initial checks in disputing a definition. Step one determines whether the definition is the provider's own or transmitted; if transmitted without commitment, only authenticity needs verification. Step two looks for ambiguous or misleading language. Step three tests the definition against four standard conditions: comprehensiveness, exclusivity, no impossibility, and no greater obscurity. These steps ensure that objections are focused and efficient.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the definition is transmitted or original
Ask the provider: Did you bring this definition from an authority or is it your own? If transmitted, is he committed to its correctness?
If transmitted without commitment, the only valid objection is to challenge the transmission's authenticity.
2
If transmitted without commitment, demand proof of transmission
The objector requests evidence that the definition was correctly transmitted from an authoritative source (e.g., sanad in hadith).
3
Scan the definition for ambiguous or misleading words
Look for terms that are ambiguous (ghāmiḍ) or that suggest an incorrect meaning (mūhim). If found, request clarification from the provider.
The provider must explain his intended meaning. This step comes before testing validity.
4
Check the four validity conditions (shurūṭ ṣiḥḥa)
Test whether the definition is: (a) comprehensive (jāmiʿ) – includes all instances, (b) exclusive (māniʿ) – excludes non-instances, (c) free from impossibility (muḥāl), and (d) not more obscure than the defined (akhfā).
If any condition is violated, the objector can object on that basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أو حد ناقص له، أو قال: حدُّ الشيء الفلاني كذا، أو رسمه كذا، أو نحو ذلك، فقد اشتمل كلامه على دعوى أو دعاوى ضمنية، بأن تعريفه مؤلف من الذَّاتِيَّات، أو مؤلف من الجنس والفصل القريبين، أو ليس للشيء المعرَّف حدٌّ حقيقي تام غير هذا الحدّ، أو هذا رسم حقيقي، أو نحو ذلك. فالاعتراض عليه حينئذٍ يكون بإبطال هذه الدعاوى الضمنية، مع إقامة الدليل على إبطالها، بإثبات الدعاوى المناقضة لها. ترتيب المناظرة في التعريف تتَّبع الخطوات التالية لدى المناظرة في التعريف: أولاً: ينظر في التعريف، فإذا كان مُورده ناقلاً له عن غيره، غير ملتزم لصحته، فليس للمعترض (المستدل) إلا أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل، ومتى أثبت مُورِدُ التعريف ما يفيد أن نقله صحيح، وفق طرائق إثبات النقول والمرويات، فقد أدى ما عليه وينتهي الأمر بذلك. أما إذا جاء بالتعريف من عنده، أو ذكره دون أن ينقله عن غيره، أو كان ناقلاً له وأعلن التزامه بصحته، فإن مناقشته والاعتراض عليه تكون وفق ما يلي: ثانياً: يُنظر في التعريف، هل فيه لفظ غامض أو موهم لمعنى غير صحيح أو لا؟ فإن كان فيه شيء من ذلك كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يستفسر، أي: أن يطلب تفسير الغامض، أو بيان معنى اللفظ الذي يوهم ما لا يصح، وعلى مُورِد التعريف أن يبين مراده. وإذا لم يكن فيه شيء من ذلك، فإلى الخطوة التالية. ثالثاً: يُنظر في التعريف، هل هو مستوف شروط صحته أو لا؟ فإن كان غير مستوف لذلك، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف بموجب الخلل الذي فيه، بأنه غير جامع، أو غير مانع، أو يستلزم المحال، أو هو أخفى من المعرَّف. ويجيب صاحب التعريف بالأجوبة التي سبق بيانها. وإن كان مستوفياً لشروط صحته، فلا اعتراض عليه من هذا الوجه، وعندئذٍ تأتي الخطوة التالية. رابعاً: ينظر في التعريف، هل تضمن دعاوى ضمنية، بأنه مثلاً حقيقي أو حدّ تام، أو رسم تام، أو نحو ذلك، فإن تضمن شيئاً من ذلك، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه بادّعاء أن التعريف ليس بحقيقي، أو ليس بحد تام، أو ليس برسم تام، وعليه أن يقيم الدليل على ذلك، بما ينتج دعواه، وتجري المناظرة على أساس دعوى المعترض (المستدل)، وفق خطوات المناظرة في التصديقات. خامساً: ثم ينظر في التعريف، هل هو مستوف لشروط حسنة أو لا؟ فإذا كان مستوفياً لشروط حسنة، فلا مجال للاعتراض عليه من هذا الوجه، وإن كان غير مستوف لشروط حسنة، كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يوجه اعتراضه على التعريف، بموجب ما رأى فيه من إخلال بشروط حسنة، ويجيب صاحب التعريف (المانع) بالأجوبة التي سبق بيانها.
Claims include refuting the implicit claims contained in the definition. The definition provider may respond by denying (al-man') the evidence of the arguer (al-mustadill), whether he mentions the evidence for denial or not. So if the definition provider says, for example: 'This definition of mine is a complete definition (hadd tāmm) of the defined, or an incomplete definition (hadd nāqiṣ) of it', or he says: 'The definition of such-and-such thing is such-and-such, or its description (rasm) is such-and-such', or similar, then his speech contains a claim or implicit claims, such that his definition is composed of essential predicates (al-dhātiyyāt), or composed of the proximate genus and differentia, or that the defined thing has no other true complete definition besides this one, or that this is a true description, or similar. So the objection against him then is to invalidate these implicit claims, along with providing evidence for their invalidation, by proving the contradictory claims. The order of disputation in definition follows the following steps in disputation over a definition: First: The definition is examined. If its provider is transmitting it from someone else, not committed to its correctness, then the objector (arguer) may only demand proof of the correctness of the transmission. And whenever the definition provider proves that his transmission is correct, according to the methods of verifying transmissions and narrations, he has fulfilled his duty and the matter ends there. However, if he brings the definition from himself, or mentions it without transmitting it from someone else, or if he transmits it but declares his commitment to its correctness, then its discussion and objection are according to what follows: Second: The definition is examined: does it contain an ambiguous word or one that suggests an incorrect meaning or not? If it contains any of that, the objector (arguer) may inquire, i.e., request an explanation of the ambiguous part, or clarification of the meaning of the word that suggests what is not correct. And the definition provider must clarify his intended meaning. If it does not contain any such thing, then to the next step. Third: The definition is examined: does it fulfill the conditions of its correctness or not? If it does not fulfill them, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection against the definition based on the defect it contains, such as being not comprehensive (ghayr jāmi'), or not exclusive (ghayr māni'), or entailing impossibility (muḥāl), or being more obscure than the defined. And the definition provider responds with the answers that have been previously explained. If it fulfills the conditions of its correctness, then there is no objection from this aspect, and then the next step comes. Fourth: The definition is examined: does it contain implicit claims, such as that it is a real (ḥaqīqī) definition, or a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm), or a complete description (rasm tāmm), or the like? If it contains any of that, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection by claiming that the definition is not real, or not a complete definition, or not a complete description, and he must provide evidence for that, which yields his claim. And the disputation proceeds on the basis of the claim of the objector (arguer), according to the steps of disputation in assents (taṣdīqāt). Fifth: Then the definition is examined: does it fulfill the conditions of a good definition (shurūṭ ḥusn) or not? If it fulfills the conditions of goodness, then there is no room for objection from this aspect. If it does not fulfill the conditions of goodness, the objector (arguer) may direct his objection against the definition based on what he sees as a violation of the conditions of goodness, and the definition provider (al-māni') responds with the answers that have been previously explained.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the procedural order for disputing definitions in dialectical debate (jadal). It begins by explaining that definitions often carry implicit claims (daʿāwā ḍimniyya), such as the claim that the definition is composed of essential attributes or includes the proximate genus and differentia. The objector's role is to refute these implicit claims with evidence, while the definition provider may simply deny (manʿ) the evidence without necessarily providing counterevidence. The heart of the passage is a five-step sequence for examining a definition. Step one checks whether the definition is transmitted from an authority or the provider's own. If transmitted without commitment to its correctness, only the transmission's authenticity needs verification; otherwise, the full procedure applies. Step two checks for ambiguous or misleading wording; the objector may request clarification. Step three tests the definition against standard validity conditions: it must be comprehensive (jāmiʿ), exclusive (māniʿ), not entail impossibility, and not be more obscure than the defined. Step four examines implicit claims like being a 'true definition' or 'complete definition'; the objector must provide proof that the definition falls short. Step five evaluates the definition's aesthetic qualities (shurūṭ ḥusn), such as clarity, conciseness, and avoidance of superfluous terms. The passage is a practical guide for students learning the art of dialectic, showing how to systematically challenge a definition without overlooking any aspect.

Key Terms
شروط صحة التعريف
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should the objector do first if the definition provider has brought the definition from himself?
A) Demand proof of transmission.
B) Check for ambiguous words.
C) Test validity conditions.
D) Claim implicit claims.

Key Takeaway
The first three steps—transmission check, ambiguity clarification, and validity conditions—sequentially narrow the grounds for legitimate objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. A student defines 'fish' as 'an aquatic animal'. Apply steps 1-3 to this definition: Is it transmitted? Is there any ambiguous term? Check jāmiʿ (does it cover all fish?) and māniʿ (does it exclude dolphins?).
?
2. In a debate, one person gives a definition of 'salah' as 'specific movements and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. The opponent objects that it is not comprehensive (does not include the intention). Which step of the procedure permits this objection?

Advanced Objections: Implicit Claims and Conditions of Goodness
Author explains steps four and five: implicit claims and conditions of goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify implicit claims such as 'real definition' or 'complete definition' and evaluate whether a definition meets the conditions of goodness.

Core Idea
Beyond validity, definitions make implicit claims of completeness; and even valid definitions can be critiqued for lack of elegance.

This lesson covers the final two steps of the disputation procedure. Step four examines whether the definition includes implicit claims beyond the basic validity conditions, such as being a 'true definition' (ḥaqīqī) or a 'complete definition' (ḥadd tāmm). The objector can challenge these claims with evidence. Step five introduces the 'conditions of goodness' (shurūṭ ḥusn), which include clarity, conciseness, and avoidance of figurative language. Even a valid definition can be rejected if it fails these aesthetic standards.

Guided Path
1
Identify implicit claims beyond validity
Examine the definition for claims that go beyond the basic conditions. Examples: 'This is the real definition' (ḥaqīqī) or 'This is a complete definition' (ḥadd tāmm) imply that it captures the essence perfectly.
These claims are often unstated; the objector must infer them from context.
2
Formulate an objection targeting an implicit claim with evidence
Once you identify an implicit claim, object by claiming the opposite (e.g., 'This definition is not real because it includes accidental properties'). Provide evidence from the definition itself or external examples.
The objector bears the burden of proof here.
3
Check the definition against conditions of goodness (shurūṭ ḥusn)
Assess whether the definition is clear (not ambiguous), concise (not verbose), free from figurative language, and does not use obscure terms. If any condition is violated, object accordingly.
These conditions are about style and clarity, not correctness.
