The Mustadil: Objector in Division Debates
Author defines 'mustadil' as the objector in division debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the 'mustadil' in a division-based debate and distinguish it from the standard usage of the term.

Core Idea
In jadal on divisions, the objector is a 'mustadil' who demands proof, not just a critic.

This lesson introduces a key term in the rules of debate (munazara) concerning logical divisions. The passage specifies that the person who raises an objection against a proposed division is called the 'mustadil' (literally, one who seeks evidence). This technical usage may differ from other contexts where 'mustadil' refers to the one providing evidence. Understanding this shift is essential for following the structured dialogue of Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term 'mustadil' in the Arabic.
Find the word 'مستدل' in the passage. Note that it is placed in parentheses, indicating it is a technical term being defined.
2
Identify the author's definition.
The author says 'الذي يُوجّه الاعتراض على التقسيم' – the one who directs the objection to the division. This is the definition of 'mustadil' in this context.
3
Compare with standard jadal usage.
In many jadal texts, 'mustadil' refers to the one who provides evidence (dalil). Here, it is used for the objector who demands evidence. This role reversal is a contextual shift.
Be alert for this shift when reading other sources.
4
Understand the function in a debate.
The mustadil challenges the division by pointing out deficiencies or demanding proof. This initiates the dialectical exchange where the proponent must defend the division.

Source Text

ضوابط المناظرة في التقسيمات أ ـ الذي يُوجّه الاعتراض على التقسيم يطلق عليه هنا اسم (مستدل)،
Rules of debate in divisions: A. He who directs the objection to the division is called here the 'mustadil' (one who seeks evidence).
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage begins a section on the rules of debate (munazara) specifically concerning divisions. It states that the person who raises an objection to a proposed division is termed the 'mustadil' (literally, the one who seeks evidence or proof). This is a technical term in jadal (dialectic) where the objector is not simply a critic but one who demands justification. The passage sets the stage for further rules on how objections and responses should be conducted in the context of logical divisions.

Key Takeaway

Show me that your division is complete.' Identify which speaker is acting as the mustadil and explain why.

Rules for Objection and Response in Division Debates
Author lists rules for objecting to and defending divisions in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid objections to a division in a dialectical debate and distinguish the roles of mustadil and mani'.

Core Idea
Objector must prove; defender may block a premise without proof.

In Islamic dialectical tradition, divisions are foundational for analysis. This passage from Dawabit al-Ma'rifa specifies the rules for debating divisions: the objector (mustadil) must bring proof for his objection, while the defender (mani') may simply block a premise. Understanding these roles and the types of objections for different divisions is essential for engaging in rigorous munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
Locate the definitions of mustadil (objector) and mani' (defender) in the passage. Note that the objector must always present proof, while the defender may block without proof.
2
Distinguish the two types of division
The passage separately treats division of a whole into parts and division of a universal into particulars. Recognize the difference: parts of a whole are spatial/structural, while particulars are instances of a concept.
3
Learn objections for whole-part divisions
There are two permissible objections: (1) the division is not exhaustive (either missing some parts or including non-parts), and (2) the parts are not mutually exclusive (or one part overlaps with the whole).
4
Learn objections for universal-particular divisions
Three objections: (1) non-exhaustiveness (by supplying another rational or existent particular, or showing a listed item is not a true particular), (2) one division is not absolutely more specific (i.e., not a proper subclass), (3) divisions overlap.
5
Understand the defender's response
The mani' may block any single premise of the mustadil's argument. He does not need to provide his own proof; blocking is sufficient to repel the objection.

Source Text

ضوابط المناظرة في التقسيمات أ ـ الذي يُوجّه الاعتراض على التقسيم يطلق عليه هنا اسم (مستدل)، وصاحب التقسيم أو الذي ينصب نفسه للدفاع عنه، ولبيان سقوط الاعتراض، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع). ب - يُوجّه الاعتراض على التقسيم بما يخالف شروطه التي سبق بيانها. (فللمستدل) أن يعترض على تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه بأحد اعتراضين: الأول: بأن التقسيم غير حاصر، إما لأنه غير جامع لكل أقسام المَقْسِم، وإما لأنه غير مانع من دخول غير أقسامه فيه. الثاني: بأن الأقسام غير متباينة، أو أحدها غير مباين للمَقْسِم. (وللمستدل) أن يعترض على تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته بأحد الاعتراضات الثلاثة التالية: الأول: بأن التقسيم غير حاصر، وذلك بأن يذكر المستدل قسماً آخر يُجيزه العقل، إن كان التقسيم عقلياً، أو يذكر قسماً متحقق الوقوع في الخارج، إن كان التقسيم استقرائياً، أو يبين أن أحد الأقسام التي ذكرها صاحب التقسيم، ليس جزئياً من جزئيات الكلي، فالتقسيم غير مانع من دخول ما ليس من جزئيات المَقْسِم. الثاني: بأن أحد الأقسام ليس أخص مطلقاً من المَقْسِم. الثالث: بأن الأقسام غير متباينة. جـ - والأجوبة التي يدفع بها (المانع) صاحب التقسيم، الاعتراضات التي يوجهها (المستدل) على التقسيم، ترجع إلى ما يطلقون عليه عبارة: (١) إنما أطلق على موجه الاعتراض هنا كموجه الاعتراض على التعريف اسم (مستدل)، للإشعار بأن الذي ينقض التقسيم أو التعريف، لا يجوز له أن ينقضه إلا مع ذكر الدليل على صحة ما يذكره من أوجه النقض، فهو بهذا مستدل يقدم الدليل على ما يدعي من النقض، وصاحب التقسيم أو التعريف يكفيه أن يجيب بمنع إحدى مقدمات دليل المعترض، سواء أذكر مع منعه سنداً أو لا.
Rules of debate concerning divisions: A. The one who directs an objection to the division is called here the 'mustadil' (arguer), and the one who makes the division or who sets himself to defend it and to show the failure of the objection is called the 'mani'' (preventer). B. The objection is directed at the division by [pointing out] violation of its conditions previously stated. For the mustadil, he may object to the division of a whole into its parts by one of two objections: First, that the division is not exhaustive, either because it does not encompass all divisions of the divided, or because it does not prevent the entry of what is not among its divisions. Second, that the divisions are not mutually exclusive, or one of them is not exclusive of the divided. For the mustadil, he may object to the division of a universal into its particulars by one of the three following objections: First, that the division is not exhaustive, such that the mustadil mentions another division that reason allows, if the division is rational, or mentions a division that actually occurs in external existence, if the division is inductive, or shows that one of the divisions mentioned by the maker of the division is not a particular among the particulars of the universal, so the division does not prevent entry of what is not among the particulars of the divided. Second, that one of the divisions is not absolutely more specific than the divided. Third, that the divisions are not mutually exclusive. C. The answers by which the mani', the maker of the division, repels the objections directed by the mustadil against the division go back to what they refer to as: (1) He gave the name 'mustadil' to the one directing the objection here, just like the one directing the objection to the definition, to indicate that the one who refutes the division or definition is not allowed to refute it except with mention of proof for the validity of what he mentions of the aspects of refutation. Thus he is a mustadil who presents proof for what he claims of refutation, while the maker of the division or definition suffices by answering with the prevention (man') of one of the premises of the objector's proof, whether he mentions with his prevention a basis or not.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage lays out the rules (dawabit) for conducting a debate (munazara) focused on divisions. It defines the roles: the objector is called 'mustadil' (one who brings a proof) and the defender is called 'mani'' (one who prevents/block). The mustadil's objections must target the conditions of correct division. Two types of division are considered: division of a whole into its parts, and division of a universal into its particulars. For the first type, there are two possible objections: (1) the division is not exhaustive (ghayr hasir) – either it misses some parts (not jami') or it allows entry of what is not a part (not mani'); (2) the parts are not mutually exclusive (mutabayina) or one part is not exclusive of the whole (i.e., it does not fully constitute the whole). For the second type (universal to particulars), there are three objections: (1) non-exhaustiveness – either the objector supplies another allowable division (rational) or an actually existing one (inductive), or shows that one mentioned is not a true particular; (2) a division is not absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) than the universal; (3) divisions overlap. The passage then notes that the answers of the mani' ultimately reduce to the concept of 'man'' (blocking a premise), and a footnote explains why the objector is called 'mustadil': he must always bring a proof for his objection, while the defender may simply block a premise without necessarily providing a proof.

Key Terms
مستدل
مانع
تقسيم حاصر
مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Must provide a parallel proof.
B) May simply block one of the premises of the objector's argument.
C) Must accept the objection if it is rational.
D) Cannot respond without additional evidence.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debate on divisions, the objector must prove his objection, while the defender can block a premise without proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the division of 'acts' into 'wajib, mandub, haram, makruh, mubah'. Identify which type of division (whole-parts or universal-particulars) this is, then formulate one valid objection according to the rules in the passage.

In a debate, the objector says the division of 'knowledge' into 'acquired and inspired' is not exhaustive because there is a third type 'intuitive knowledge'. The defender wants to block the objection. What premise might he block, and does he need to provide a proof?

Tahreer al-Murad: Defending Divisions by Clarifying Intent
Introduces the answer technique 'tahreer al-murad' and explains its first aspect with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four aspects of tahreer al-murad and apply the first aspect (clarifying the dividend) to resolve objections in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Clarifying what you meant is the first defense against objections to your division.

In dialectical reasoning, objections often arise because the objector understands a term differently than the defender intended. The technique of 'tahreer al-murad' (clarifying the intended meaning) is the primary method to defuse such objections without conceding the division. This lesson introduces the four aspects of tahreer al-murad and examines the first aspect in depth with concrete examples from 'aqeeda and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's misunderstanding
Read the division and the objection. Determine where the objector's interpretation diverges from what the defender likely intended. In the first example, the objector assumed 'correct beliefs' includes sensory beliefs.
The objection must stem from a different understanding of a term, not from a genuine flaw in the division.
2
Locate the source of misunderstanding
Determine which part of the division caused the confusion: the dividend (maqsim), the divisions (aqsam), the division operation itself, or the underlying doctrine. Here, the confusion is about the dividend 'correct beliefs'.
The four aspects correspond to these four sources.
3
For the dividend case, specify the intended scope
The defender clarifies what he meant by the dividend, narrowing or expanding its scope to exclude the omitted items. In the example, he restricts 'correct beliefs' to theoretical ones, excluding sensory intuitive beliefs.
This makes the division exhaustive with respect to the intended meaning.
4
Show how clarification repels the objection
With the clarified meaning, the objector's example no longer fits under the dividend, so the objection fails. The division stands as defen proposed.
This technique does not require changing the division, only clarifying terms.

Source Text

خمس صلوات في اليوم والليلة.
C - And the answers by which the defender, the author of the division, repels the objections that the interrogator directs against the division, go back to what they call the expression: 'Clarifying the intended meaning', and that is by explaining his intended meaning of what was the cause of the objection, so that by explaining his intended meaning he corrects what the interrogator understood from his speech so that he directed his objection because of it. And the clarification of the intended meaning here is in four ways: First way: Clarifying the intended meaning from the dividend. Second way: Clarifying the intended meaning from the divisions. Third way: Clarifying the intended meaning from the division. Fourth way: Clarifying the intended meaning from the scientific doctrine on which the division is based. Clarifying the intended meaning from the dividend: The division may differ according to the difference in the intended meaning of the dividend, so the interrogator's objection arises according to his understanding contrary to the defender's intended meaning; then his answer is by clarifying his intended meaning from the dividend, and thereby the objection is repelled. Examples: 1 - The author of the division presented a division of correct beliefs, mentioning that they are divided into two types: rational and traditional. The interrogator objected that this division is non-exhaustive, because beliefs based on direct sensory perception are excluded. The defender answered by clarifying his intended meaning from the dividend, saying: I only meant theoretical beliefs, not intuitive beliefs, and things perceived by direct sense are among the intuitive, not the theoretical, therefore I reject your objection. 2 - The jurist, author of the division, presented a division of obligatory prayer, saying: it is five prayers in the day and night.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage explains a key dialectical technique called 'tahreer al-murad' (clarifying the intended meaning). It is the primary answer method for an author of a division (al-mani') to counter objections. The defender clarifies what he actually meant by a term or the division itself, showing that the objection was based on a misunderstanding. Four aspects of clarification are listed: clarifying the intended meaning of (1) the dividend (al-maqsim), (2) the divisions (al-aqsam), (3) the division (al-taqseem) itself, and (4) the underlying scientific doctrine (al-madhhab al-'ilmi). Then the passage focuses on the first aspect with two examples. In the first example, someone divides 'correct beliefs' into rational and traditional. An objector says this misses sensory beliefs. The defender clarifies that he meant only theoretical beliefs (al-'aqa'id al-nazariyya), not intuitive ones (al-badihiyya), thus the objection is invalid. In the second example, a jurist divides obligatory prayer into five daily prayers. The passage cuts off, but the point is that objectors might misunderstand what 'obligatory prayer' includes. The technique shows the importance of precise definitions in dialectical exchanges.

B) Clarifying the intended meaning of the dividend.

Key Takeaway
Tahreer al-murad min al-maqsim allows a defender to preserve a division by specifying the intended scope of the dividend, repelling objections based on misinterpretation.

Clarifying the Category Being Divided in Dialectic
Introduces the method of clarifying the category being divided to refute objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of clarifying the intended meaning of the category being divided (tahrīr al-murād min al-maqsim) to refute objections against a classification.

Core Idea
Clarifying what you mean by the category being divided can resolve objections without changing the division.

In dialectical debates, objections often arise from misunderstanding the scope of the category being divided. This lesson teaches the first of four methods used by the preventer (al-māni‘) to respond: clarifying the intended meaning of the category (al-maqsim). By precisely defining the category, the objection is shown to be based on a misinterpretation, thus preserving the original division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification and the objection
Locate the original division and the proponent's complaint that it is not exhaustive. For example, a division of correct beliefs into rational and scriptural is objected to for excluding sensory beliefs.
2
Determine the misunderstood category
Find which category (al-maqsim) is being misinterpreted by the objector. Here it is 'ʿaqāʾid ṣaḥīḥa' (correct beliefs).
3
Clarify the intended meaning
State explicitly what the author meant by that category, usually by specifying a subset or condition. The preventer says he meant 'theoretical beliefs' (nazariyyāt), not 'intuitive beliefs' (badīhiyyāt).
4
Show that the excluded item falls outside this intended meaning
Demonstrate that the objection is invalid because the item does not belong to the clarified category. Sensory beliefs are intuitive, so they are excluded from the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تحرير المراد)، وذلك ببيان مراده مما كان سبب الاعتراض، فيصحح ببيان مراده ما فهمه المستدل من كلامه فَوجَّهَ له اعتراضه بسببه. ويكون تحرير المراد هنا بأربعة وجوه: الوجه الأول: تحرير المراد من المَقْسِم. الوجه الثاني: تحرير المراد من الأقسام. الوجه الثالث: تحرير المراد من التقسيم. الوجه الرابع: تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التقسيم. تحرير المراد من المَقْسِم قد يختلف التقسيم باختلاف المراد من (المَقْسِم)، فيتوجه اعتراض (المستدل) وفق فهمه المخالف لمراد (المانع) صاحب التقسيم، عندئذ يكون جوابه بتحرير مراده من المَقْسِم، وبذلك يندفع الاعتراض. أمثلة : ١ - أورد صاحب التقسيم تقسيماً للعقائد الصحيحة، فذكر أنها تنقسم إلى قسمين: عقلية وخبرية. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لخروج العقائد المستندة إلى الإدراك الحسي المباشر. فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من المَقْسِم فقال: إنما أردت العقائد النظرية، لا العقائد البدهية، والمدركاتُ بالحس المباشر من البدهيات لا من النظريات، لذلك أمنع اعتراضك. ٢ - أورد الفقيه صاحب التقسيم تقسيماً للصلاة المفروضة فقال: هي خمس صلوات في اليوم والليلة. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لخروج الصلاة على الميت، فهي صلاة مفروضة. فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من المقسم فقال: إنما أردت الصلاة المفروضة فرضاً عينياً لا فرضاً كفائياً، لذلك أمنع اعتراضك.
(Clarifying the intended meaning): that is by explaining his intended meaning from what was the cause of the objection, so by explaining his intended meaning he corrects what the proponent understood from his speech, and thus the proponent directed his objection because of it. And the clarifying of the intended meaning here occurs in four ways: The first way: clarifying the intended meaning from the category being divided. The second way: clarifying the intended meaning from the subdivisions. The third way: clarifying the intended meaning from the division itself. The fourth way: clarifying the intended meaning from the scientific doctrine upon which the division is based. Clarifying the intended meaning from the category being divided: Sometimes the division differs according to the difference in the intended meaning of the category being divided, so the objection of the proponent is directed according to his understanding contrary to the intended meaning of the preventer, the author of the division. At that point his response is to clarify his intended meaning from the category being divided, and thereby the objection is repelled. Examples: 1. The author of a division presented a division of correct beliefs, stating that they divide into two types: rational and scriptural. The proponent objected that this division is not exhaustive, because beliefs based on direct sensory perception are excluded. The preventer responded by clarifying his intended meaning from the category being divided, saying: I meant only theoretical beliefs, not intuitive beliefs, and things perceived by direct sense are among the intuitive, not the theoretical, therefore I deny your objection. 2. The jurist, the author of a division, presented a division of obligatory prayer, saying: it is five prayers in the day and night. The proponent objected that this division is not exhaustive, because the funeral prayer is excluded, as it is an obligatory prayer. The preventer responded by clarifying his intended meaning from the category being divided, saying: I meant only the prayer obligatory as a personal obligation, not as a communal obligation, therefore I deny your objection.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is dialectical, explaining the first method (of four) for responding to objections against a classification: clarifying what the author meant by the category being divided (al-maqsim). The passage first introduces the concept of tahrir al-murad (clarifying the intended meaning) as a way to correct misunderstandings that lead to objections. It then lists four ways, but only elaborates the first. The first way states that the division may vary according to the intended meaning of the divided category; if a proponent objects based on his own understanding, the preventer can respond by clarifying what he actually meant by the category. Two examples from Islamic sciences illustrate this: (1) A division of correct beliefs into rational and scriptural is objected to for excluding sensory beliefs, but the author clarifies he meant only theoretical beliefs; sensory beliefs are intuitive and not part of the division. (2) A division of obligatory prayer into five daily prayers is objected to for excluding the funeral prayer, but the author clarifies he meant personally obligatory prayers, not communally obligatory ones. Thus, by clarifying the scope of the divided category, the objection is repelled.

المَقْسِم
المستدل
المانع

Method 2: Clarifying Intended Meaning of Subdivisions
Explains the second method: clarifying the intended meaning of the subdivisions to rebut objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of clarifying intended meaning of subdivisions to defend a classification against the objection of incompleteness by redefining a term.

Core Idea
A missing subdivision may only be missing in name; clarifying the intended scope of existing subdivisions can save a classification.

In dialectical debates over classifications, an opponent may object that a classification excludes a key category. Instead of adding a new subdivision, the proponent can clarify that a particular subdivision was intended broadly enough to include the apparently missing items. This lesson walks through the logic of this rebuttal using two legal examples from fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification and the objection
Find the original division (e.g., contracts: sale, loan, rental, gift, partnership) and the opponent's claim that it excludes an important category (e.g., riba contracts).
The objection is that the division is non-exhaustive.
2
Locate the contested subdivision
Determine which existing term the opponent thinks fails to cover the missing category. In the example, 'loan' and 'sale' are the relevant terms.
The opponent assumes these have narrow technical meanings.
3
Determine if a broader interpretation is plausible
Check whether the term can legitimately be understood more broadly in the context. For instance, 'loan' can include both qard hasan and ribawi loans; 'sale' can include exchanges of like goods (riba al-fadl).
This step requires knowledge of the domain.
4
Formulate the clarification
State explicitly that by the term you meant the broader sense. For example: 'By loan, I mean any transfer of ownership with a condition to return, including interest-based ones.'
This is the key rebuttal move.
5
Verify the classification is now exhaustive
Confirm that with the clarified meanings, all instances of the supposedly missing category fall under one of the existing subdivisions. In the example, all riba contracts are now included under loan or sale.
The method preserves the original structure.

تحرير المراد من الأقسام قد يتوجه الاعتراض على التقسيم بسبب اختلاف فهم (المستدل) لبعض الأقسام عن فهم (المانع ) صاحب التقسيم، عندئذ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من القسم الذي فهمه المستدل على خلاف مراده منه. أمثلة : ١ - أورد صاحب التقسيم تقسيماً للعقود المالية فقال: تنقسم إلى بيع وقرض وإجارة وهبة وشركة. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لخروج عقد الربا منه. فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من بعض الأقسام فقال: أردت بالقرض ما يشمل القرض الحسن، والقرض بفائدة وهو من أنواع الربا، وأردت بالبيع ما يشمل بيع الأجناس بأمثالها، فيدخل فيه بقية أنواع الربا. ٢ - أورد صاحب التقسيم تقسيماً للأحكام الشرعية فقال: هي إما واجب، وإما جائز، وإما حرام. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لخروج المندوب والمكروه منه، وهما من الأحكام الشرعية. فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من بعض الأقسام فقال: لم أرد بالجائز ما هو مستوي الطرفين فقط، وإنما أردت به ما يشمل مستوي الطرفين، وجائز
Clarifying the intended meaning from the subdivisions: Sometimes the objection is directed against the division because of a difference in the understanding of the proponent of some of the subdivisions from the understanding of the preventer, the author of the division. At that point the response of the preventer is to clarify his intended meaning from the subdivision which the proponent understood contrary to his intended meaning. Examples: 1. The author of a division presented a division of financial contracts, saying: they divide into sale, loan, rental, gift, and partnership. The proponent objected that this division is not exhaustive, because the contract of riba (usury) is excluded from it. The preventer responded by clarifying his intended meaning from some of the subdivisions, saying: I meant by 'loan' that which includes the goodly loan and the loan with interest, which is a type of riba, and I meant by 'sale' that which includes the sale of goods for their equivalents, so the remaining types of riba enter into it. 2. The author of a division presented a division of legal rulings, saying: they are either obligatory, or permissible, or prohibited. The proponent objected that this division is not exhaustive, because the recommended (mandub) and the disliked (makruh) are excluded from it, and they are among the legal rulings. The preventer responded by clarifying his intended meaning from some of the subdivisions, saying: I did not mean by 'permissible' only that which is balanced in its two ends, but rather I meant by it that which includes the balanced and the permissible...
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage explains the second method of clarifying the intended meaning: clarifying the intended meaning of the subdivisions (al-aqsam). The passage states that an objection may arise because the proponent understands a subdivision differently than the author intended. The response is to clarify what the author meant by that particular subdivision. Two examples are given, though the second is incomplete. In the first example, a division of financial contracts into sale, loan, rental, gift, and partnership is objected to for excluding riba (usury) contracts. The author clarifies that by 'loan' he includes both interest-free and interest-based loans, and by 'sale' he includes exchanges of like goods, thus covering all forms of riba. In the second example (incomplete), a division of legal rulings into obligatory, permissible, and prohibited is objected to for excluding recommended (mandub) and disliked (makruh). The author begins his response by stating that by 'permissible' he does not mean only the neutral (al-mustawi al-tarafayn) but a broader category that includes... (text cuts off). The method shows that redefinition of a term can save a classification from the charge of incompleteness.

Clarifying the Type of Division in Dialectic
Author explains the jadal technique of tahrir al-murad from the type of division with one example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational, real, inductive, and conventional divisions, and apply the technique of tahrir al-murad to resolve an objection about exhaustiveness in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
The type of division determines whether it must be exhaustive by reason or by observation.

In dialectical debates, a proponent may present a division of a concept into categories. If the opponent misunderstands the type of division—thinking it is rationally necessary when it is actually empirical—they may object that it is not exhaustive. The proponent can then use the technique of tahrir al-murad: clarifying that the division is of a different type, thereby defusing the objection. This lesson explores this technique through an example from human classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of division intended
Read the proponent's division and note the form. Does it resemble a rational division (mutually exclusive by logical necessity) or an inductive one (based on observation)? The example uses 'either...or', which suggests a rational division, but the proponent actually intends an inductive division.
The form may mislead; the intended type is key.
2
Recognize the opponent's objection
The opponent objects that the division is not exhaustive because reason can conceive of a fourth category. This objection assumes the division is rational. Understand that the opponent is applying the wrong standard of exhaustiveness.
Objection only valid if the division claims rational necessity.
3
Apply tahrir al-murad to respond
The proponent clarifies that the division is inductive (or conventional), not rational. Since induction shows only three categories exist in reality, the division is exhaustive by observation. This resolves the objection by correcting the misunderstanding.
The response shifts the criterion from reason to reality.
4
Generalise the technique
In any dialectical exchange, if an objection hinges on the type of division, identify whether the division is aqli, haqiqi, istiqra'i, or i'tibari, and respond accordingly by clarifying the intended type.
This technique is part of adab al-bahth (dialectical ethics).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحرير المراد من التقسيم
قد يتوجه الاعتراض على التقسيم بسبب اختلاف فهم (المستدل) لنوع التقسيم، عن مراد (المانع) صاحب التقسيم، فيظن المستدل أن التقسيم مثلاً هو من نوع التقسيم العقلي، أو من نوع التقسيم الحقيقي، فيوجه اعتراضه مع أنه في مراد صاحب التقسيم، من نوع التقسيم الاستقرائي، أو من نوع التقسيم الاعتباري، عندئذٍ يكون جواب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من نوع التقسيم.
أمثلة :
١ - أورد صاحب التقسيم تقسيماً لأصناف الإنسان من جهة الذكورة والأنوثة فقال : الإنسان إما ذكر وإما لا، والثاني إما أنثى وإما لا، والثالث هو الخنثى، فالأصناف ثلاثة.
ونظراً إلى أنه أورد التقسيم بصيغة التقسيم العقلي أوهم أنه يقسم تقسيماً عقلياً.
فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لأن العقل لا يمنع من وجود قسم رابع.
فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من التقسيم فقال للمستدل : أمنع اعتراضك، لأن هذا التقسيم هو من قبيل التقسيم الاستقرائي، لا من قبيل التقسيم العقلي، فهو بذلك تقسيم حاصر، لأنه لا يوجد في الواقع غير هذه الأصناف الثلاثة للإنسان.
Clarifying the intended meaning from the [type of] division. The objection may arise against the division due to a difference between the understanding of the objector of the type of division and the intended meaning of the defender, the originator of the division. So the objector thinks that the division is, for example, of the type of rational division or real division, and thus directs his objection, while according to the originator's intent, it is of the type of inductive division or conventional division. Then the response of the defender is by clarifying his intended meaning from the type of division. Examples: 1 - The originator of the division presented a division of the kinds of human with respect to masculinity and femininity, saying: Human is either male or not, and the second is either female or not, and the third is the hermaphrodite; so the kinds are three. Since he presented the division in the form of a rational division, he gave the impression that he is dividing rationally. So the objector objected that this division is not exhaustive, because the mind does not prevent the existence of a fourth type. The defender responded by clarifying his intended meaning from the division, saying to the objector: I reject your objection, because this division is of the type of inductive division, not of the type of rational division; thus it is an exhaustive division, because in reality there are no other than these three kinds of human.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains another dialectical technique: clarifying the type of division (tahrir al-murad min al-taqseem). The objector may misunderstand the type of division being used—thinking it is a rational or real division when in fact the proponent intended an inductive or conventional division. The proponent then clarifies the type to show that the objection is invalid. The example given is a division of humans by sex: male, female, and hermaphrodite. It is presented in the form of a rational division (using 'either...or'), leading the objector to think it is a necessary rational classification, and he objects that reason does not preclude a fourth category. The defender clarifies that it is actually an inductive division based on empirical observation; since only three types exist in reality, the division is exhaustive. This shows that the apparent form of a division may mislead, and clarifying the intended type resolves the objection.

Key Terms
تقسيم عقلي
تقسيم حقيقي
تقسيم استقرائي
تقسيم اعتباري
حاصر

A) Because reason requires exactly three sexes.
B) Because induction shows no fourth type exists in reality.
C) Because the form of the division was rational.
D) Because the objector was mistaken about the number.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, clarifying the intended type of division (tahrir al-murad) resolves objections about exhaustiveness by shifting the criterion from rational necessity to empirical observation or convention.

?
In a dialectical debate, a scholar divides the causes of abrogation (naskh) into three: abrogation of ruling only, of text only, or of both. The opponent objects that reason allows more possibilities. Compose the defender's response using tahrir al-murad, specifying the type of division and why it is exhaustive.

Resolving Objections by Clarifying the Doctrinal School
Author explains how clarifying the doctrinal school underlying a division resolves objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection based on a different doctrinal school is invalid and construct a response that clarifies one's own school.

Core Idea
A division is only valid within its own doctrinal framework; cross-school objections miss the point.

In dialectical debates, a division (taqsīm) is often built on premises from a specific academic or legal school (madhhab 'ilmī). An objector may challenge the division based on a different school, but the divider can defuse the objection by clarifying his own school. This lesson teaches you to recognize such cross-school objections and respond effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divider's school
Look at the division and determine which school's principles it assumes. For example, in the fiqh example, the divider follows a school that does not consider touching women or eating camel meat as nullifiers of wudu'.
The school may be explicit or implicit.
2
Identify the objector's school
The objector's challenge reveals his own school. He assumes different nullifiers, so his school is different from the divider's.
This step helps pinpoint the source of disagreement.
3
Evaluate whether the objection is based on a different school
If the objector's claim only holds in a different school, the objection is not a valid refutation of the division itself—it only shows a difference in premises.
A valid objection must either accept the divider's school or argue that the division fails even within that school.
4
Formulate the response: clarify your school
The correct response is to state explicitly the school on which the division is based. This neutralizes the objection because the objector cannot demand that your division conform to his school.
Example: 'I based my division on the school of Imam Abu Hanifa, who does not consider these as nullifiers.'
5
Recognize that this ends the objection
Once the school is clarified, the objector's objection becomes irrelevant unless he challenges the school itself. The debate then shifts to the validity of the school, not the division.
This technique saves time and keeps the discussion focused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التقسيم
قد يختلف التقسيم باختلاف المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه، فيقدم صاحب التقسيم تقسيمه وفق المذهب الذي يذهب إليه، ويعترض عليه (المستدل) وفق مذهب آخر يذهب هو إليه، عندئذ يكون جواب (المانع) صاحب التقسيم ببيان المذهب الذي بني عليه تقسيمه، ويندفع بذلك اعتراض المستدل.
أمثلة :
١ - أورد صاحب التقسيم الفقيه تقسيماً لناقض الوضوء فقال: ناقض الوضوء إما خروج شيء من أحد السبيلين، وإما نوم مستغرق فيه احتمال خروج ريح.
فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لأن لمس المرأة بشهوة ناقض أيضاً، ولأن أكل لحم الجزور ناقض أيضاً.
فأجاب (المانع) صاحب التقسيم بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه التقسيم فقال للمستدل: أمنع اعتراضك لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب من لا يعتبر لمس النساء ناقضاً للوضوء، ولا يعتبر أكل لحم الجزور ناقضاً.
٢ - أورد صاحب التقسيم النحوي تقسيماً للكلمة فقال: الكلمة: إما اسم، وإما فعل، وإما حرف.
Clarifying the intended doctrinal school upon which the division is based. The division may differ according to the differing doctrinal school upon which it is based. So the divider presents his division according to the school he follows, and the understander objects to it according to another school that he follows. At that point the divider's response is to clarify the school upon which his division is based, and thereby the understander's objection is repelled. Examples: 1. The jurist divider presented a division of the nullifiers of ablution and said: The nullifier of ablution is either the exit of something from one of the two orifices, or deep sleep in which there is a possibility of passing gas. The understander objected that this division is not exhaustive, because touching a woman with desire is also a nullifier, and because eating camel meat is also a nullifier. The divider, the one who presented the division, responded by clarifying his intended doctrinal school upon which he based the division and said to the understander: I deny your objection because I built my division on the school of those who do not consider touching women to be a nullifier of ablution, and do not consider eating camel meat to be a nullifier. 2. The grammarian divider presented a division of the word and said: The word is either a noun, or a verb, or a particle.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical theme by addressing another type of objection: one based on differing doctrinal schools (madhahib 'ilmiyya). The author explains that a division may be built upon a specific school, and an objector may object based on a different school. The proper response is to clarify the school underlying one's division.

Two examples are given. The first is from fiqh (Islamic jurisprudence): a jurist divides the nullifiers of wudu' into two: exiting something from the private parts (urine, feces, etc.) and deep sleep with a possibility of passing gas. An objector raises that touching a woman with desire and eating camel meat are also nullifiers according to some scholars. The divider responds by clarifying that his division is based on the school that does not consider those as nullifiers. This shows the importance of specifying one's madhhab to avoid cross-school objections.

The second example begins but is cut off in the text: a grammarian divides the word into noun, verb, and particle. This is a standard division in Arabic grammar. The text does not give the objection and response, but the pattern is clear: the objector might argue that some words are not covered, and the grammarian would clarify that his division is based on a particular grammatical school.

This passage teaches that in dialectical debate, one must be aware of the doctrinal context of a division; an objection based on a different school is not valid unless the divider claims universality.

مذهب علمي

C) Because the divider's division is mental, not real

Key Takeaway
When an objection to a division is based on a different doctrinal school, the correct response is to clarify the school on which the division is built, thereby neutralizing the objection.

Defending a Division by Clarifying Its Doctrinal Basis
Explains how a logician can rebut an objection to a division by specifying its underlying doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to respond to the objection that a division is not exhaustive by clarifying the doctrinal standpoint (madhhab 'ilmi) on which the division is built.

Core Idea
A division's validity depends on its underlying doctrine; clarifying that doctrine blocks the objection.

In dialectical debate, a common objection to a division is that it is not exhaustive (ghayr hasir). This passage teaches a powerful response: the presenter clarifies that his division is based on a specific doctrinal standpoint (madhhab 'ilmi). Once the doctrinal framework is made explicit, the objection loses its force because it assumes a different doctrine. This technique is called 'tahrir al-murad min al-madhhab al-'ilmi'. The passage also distinguishes conceptual (i'tibari) divisions from real ones, though the main focus is the doctrinal basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim that the division is not exhaustive, i.e., that it omits a valid part. Note which part the objector believes is missing.
In the examples, the objector points to additional nullifiers of wudu, verbal nouns, or the intermediate state (hal).
2
Locate the responder's move
Notice that the responder (al-mani') does not add the missing part to the division. Instead, he clarifies that the division is built on a specific doctrinal standpoint that excludes that part.
The key phrase is 'banaytu taqsīmī 'alā madhhab...'
3
Understand the type of division
Determine whether the division is conceptual (i'tibari) or real (haqiqi). In a conceptual division, parts need only be mentally distinct, even if they share an external referent. This is relevant when the objection is about real distinction.
The passage first mentions conceptual division, but the examples focus on doctrinal basis.
4
Apply the technique to each example
For each example, identify the doctrinal standpoint that justifies the division. Then check whether the objection would hold if the same doctrine were accepted.
In fiqh: Hanafi doctrine. In grammar: majority doctrine. In kalam: doctrine denying the intermediate state.

فاختل شرط التباين بين الأقسام في الخارج. فأجاب (المانع) بتحرير مراده من التقسيم فقال للمستدل: أمنع اعتراضك، لأن هذا التقسيم هو من نوع التقسيم الاعتباري لا الحقيقي، والتقسيم الاعتباري يكفي فيه أن تكون الأقسام مختلفة في العقل فقط، ولو اجتمعت في ما صدق واحد. تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه التقسيم. قد يختلف التقسيم باختلاف المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه، فيقدم صاحب التقسيم تقسيمه وفق المذهب الذي يذهب إليه، ويعترض عليه (المستدل) وفق مذهب آخر يذهب هو إليه، عندئذ يكون جواب (المانع) صاحب التقسيم ببيان المذهب الذي بني عليه تقسيمه، ويندفع بذلك اعتراض المستدل. أمثلة : ١ - أورد صاحب التقسيم الفقيه تقسيماً لناقض الوضوء فقال: ناقض الوضوء إما خروج شيء من أحد السبيلين، وإما نوم مستغرق فيه احتمال خروج ريح. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لأن لمس المرأة بشهوة ناقض أيضاً، ولأن أكل لحم الجزور ناقض أيضاً. فأجاب (المانع) صاحب التقسيم بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه التقسيم فقال للمستدل: أمنع اعتراضك لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب من لا يعتبر لمس النساء ناقضاً للوضوء، ولا يعتبر أكل لحم الجزور ناقضاً. ٢ - أورد صاحب التقسيم النحوي تقسيماً للكلمة فقال: الكلمة: إما اسم، وإما فعل، وإما حرف. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لأنه لم يذكر فيه اسم الفعل، وهو قسم من أقسام الكلمة. فأجاب (المانع) مورد التقسيم بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه التقسيم، فقال للمستدل: أمنع اعتراضك لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب الجمهور، ولم أبنه على مذهب الفراء، ولا على مذهب أبي جعفر بن صابر. ٣ - أورد صاحب التقسيم المتكلم في الفلسفة تقسيماً للمعلوم فقال: المعلوم إما موجود، وإما معدوم. فاعترض (المستدل) بأن هذا التقسيم غير حاصر، لأنه لم يذكر فيه الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، والتي تسمى حالاً. فأجاب (المانع) مورد التقسيم بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه التقسيم فقال للمستدل: أمنع اعتراضك، لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب من ينكر الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، ويرى أن المعلوم إما هو موجود وإما لا، والثاني هو المعدوم.
Then the condition of distinction between the divisions in external reality is violated. So the blocker (defender) responded by clarifying his intention from the division and said to the objector: I reject your objection, because this division is of the type of conceptual (i'tibari) division, not real (haqiqi) division, and for conceptual division it suffices that the divisions are different in the mind only, even if they coincide in a single external referent. Clarifying the intention of the doctrinal standpoint (madhhab 'ilmi) upon which the division is built. The division may differ according to the doctrinal standpoint upon which it is built. So the presenter of the division presents his division according to the doctrine he holds, and the objector objects to it according to another doctrine that he holds. At that point, the response of the blocker, the presenter of the division, is to clarify the doctrine upon which his division is built, and thereby the objection of the objector is repelled. Examples: 1. The jurist presenter of a division presented a division of the nullifiers of wudu and said: The nullifier of wudu is either the exit of something from one of the two passages, or deep sleep where there is possibility of passing wind. So the objector objected that this division is not exhaustive, because touching a woman with desire is also a nullifier, and because eating camel meat is also a nullifier. So the blocker, the presenter of the division, responded by clarifying his intention of the doctrinal standpoint upon which he built the division, and said to the objector: I reject your objection because I built my division on the doctrine of one who does not consider touching women to be a nullifier of wudu, and does not consider eating camel meat to be a nullifier. 2. The grammarian presenter of a division presented a division of the word and said: The word is either a noun, or a verb, or a particle. So the objector objected that this division is not exhaustive, because it did not mention the verbal noun (ism fi'l), which is a part of speech. So the blocker, the presenter of the division, responded by clarifying his intention of the doctrinal standpoint upon which he built the division, and said to the objector: I reject your objection because I built my division on the doctrine of the majority, and I did not build it on the doctrine of al-Farra', nor on the doctrine of Abu Ja'far ibn Sabir. 3. The dialectical theologian (mutakallim) presenting a division in philosophy presented a division of the known and said: The known is either existent or non-existent. So the objector objected that this division is not exhaustive, because it did not mention the intermediate between existent and non-existent, which is called 'state' (hal). So the blocker, the presenter of the division, responded by clarifying his intention of the doctrinal standpoint upon which he built the division, and said to the objector: I reject your objection because I built my division on the doctrine of one who denies the intermediate between existent and non-existent, and holds that the known is either existent or not (non-existent).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical technique for defending a logical division against the objection that it is not exhaustive (ghayr hasir). The author first introduces the problem: an objector claims the division violates the condition of real distinction (tabayun) between the parts. The responder (al-mani') counters by clarifying that his division is conceptual (i'tibari), not real (haqiqi). In a conceptual division, it is enough that the parts are conceptually distinct, even if they share one external referent. The core of the response is 'tahrir al-murad min al-madhhab al-'ilmi'—clarifying the doctrinal standpoint on which the division is built. The author explains that a division can vary according to the doctrinal commitment of the presenter. The objector may object based on his own doctrine, but the responder points out that the division is only intended to cover the divisions as per his own school. Three examples illustrate this. The first is from fiqh: the nullifiers of wudu according to a particular school (apparently the Hanafi school, which does not consider touching women or eating camel meat as nullifiers). The second is from grammar: the division of the word into noun, verb, and particle is based on the majority view, not the views of al-Farra' or Abu Ja'far ibn Sabir who treat the verbal noun as a separate part. The third is from kalam/philosophy: the division of the known into existent and non-existent is based on the doctrine that denies any intermediate (hal) between existence and non-existence. In each case, the responder simply insists that his division is valid within his own doctrinal framework, and the objector's objection is thereby deflected. The passage teaches that divisions are not absolute but are grounded in specific theoretical commitments; a critique must engage with those commitments.

Key Terms
المانع
التقسيم الاعتباري
المذهب العلمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A division's exhaustiveness is relative to its underlying doctrine; clarifying that doctrine invalidates objections based on other doctrines.

Defending a Division: The Grammar Example
Grammar example of a division defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the respondent's defense strategy in a jadal debate when a division is accused of being non-exhaustive.

Core Idea
A division's exhaustiveness is relative to its doctrinal foundation.

In the first example, an objector claims that a division of 'word' into noun and particle is incomplete because it omits the verb. The respondent does not change the division but invokes his doctrinal school: he follows the majority (jumhur) who treat the verb as a subtype of the noun. This lesson unpacks that move and shows how the respondent's clarification neutralizes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division and the objection
The respondent divides 'word' (kalima) into noun and particle. The objector says this is not exhaustive because the verb is missing.
The objector assumes the standard three-part division (noun, verb, particle).
2
Locate the doctrinal basis (madhhab 'ilmi)
The respondent replies that he based his division on the position of the majority (jumhur). In the majority view, the verb is considered a kind of noun (because it shares some features), so the division is exhaustive within that school.
The respondent is not claiming his division is exhaustive absolutely, but within his chosen framework.
3
Assess whether the division is exhaustive within that framework
If the majority school indeed subsumes verbs under nouns, then 'noun and particle' covers all words. The objector must accept this if he engages within that framework.
The debate shifts from the division itself to the doctrinal foundation.
4
Determine the validity of the objection
The objection is valid only if both parties share the same doctrine. By explicitly stating his basis, the respondent shows the objection does not apply on his terms.
This illustrates the jadal principle that arguments must be evaluated relative to the assumed premises.

Then the objector objected that this division is not exhaustive, because it does not mention the verb, which is a part of speech. So the respondent, the one who presented the division, answered by clarifying his intended meaning from the doctrinal school upon which he based the division, saying to the objector: I deny your objection because I based my division on the position of the majority, and I did not base it on the position of al-Farra' or on the position of Abu Ja'far ibn Sabir. 3. Commentary
This passage presents two examples of dialectical exchanges concerning exhaustive divisions. In the first example, an objector (mustadill) claims that the presented division of 'word' (kalima) is not exhaustive because it omits the verb. The respondent (mani') defends by clarifying that his division is based on the majority school (jumhur) in grammar, which considers only noun and particle, and not the minority views of al-Farra' or Abu Ja'far ibn Sabir. The second example deals with a philosophical division of the known (ma'lum) into existent and non-existent. The objector objects that the intermediate 'state' (hal) is omitted. The respondent defends by stating that his division is based on the doctrine that denies the existence of an intermediate state, thus the division is exhaustive on that view. In both cases, the respondent's defense rests on clarifying the doctrinal basis (madhhab 'ilmi) upon which the division is built, showing that the objection is only valid if the objector shares that basis. The examples illustrate the jadal principle that a division must be evaluated within its own framework.

Defending a Division: The Philosophical Example
Philosophical example of a division defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector's claim of non-exhaustiveness is based on an assumed category that the respondent's doctrine rejects.

Core Idea
If the objector's omitted category is denied by the respondent's school, the division remains exhaustive.

In the second example, the division is of 'the known' into existent and non-existent. The objector introduces the concept of 'state' (hal) as an intermediate category. The respondent defends by stating his division is based on the doctrine that denies any intermediate between existence and non-existence. This lesson examines how rejecting the ontological status of an intermediate category renders the division exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division and the objection
The respondent divides the known (ma'lum) into existent and non-existent. The objector says this is not exhaustive because the 'state' (hal) is an intermediate category.
The concept of 'state' (e.g., potentiality) was debated in Islamic philosophy and kalam.
2
Locate the doctrinal basis (madhhab 'ilmi)
The respondent clarifies that his division is built on the position of those who deny any intermediate between existent and non-existent. For them, everything that is known is either actually existent or non-existent; a 'state' is not a distinct ontological category.
This corresponds to a particular theological stance (e.g., Ash'ari rejection of the 'state' as a separate category).
3
Evaluate exhaustiveness within that doctrine
If there is no intermediate, then the division into existent and non-existent is exhaustive by definition. The objector's 'state' is either reduced to one of the two or rejected as illegitimate.
The respondent is not saying the concept of state is meaningless, but that it does not belong in this division.
4
Assess the objector's position
The objector's objection only holds if the respondent shares the belief in an intermediate category. By clarifying his own doctrinal basis, the respondent shows the objection is misplaced.
This move forces the objector to either adopt the same doctrine or argue against the doctrinal basis directly.

Three-Step Procedure for Disputing a Division
Three-step procedure for disputing a division in jadal: source, clarity, and essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three-step procedure for disputing a division in jadal, distinguishing between challenges to source, clarity, and essence.

Core Idea
Before attacking a division's logic, first check its source and wording.

In jadal (dialectical debate), divisions are common tools for argumentation. However, not every division deserves immediate logical scrutiny. This lesson presents a three-step procedure that governs how objections should be raised: first challenge the source, then the clarity, and only then the essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presenter's stance
Determine if the presenter is a mere transmitter (naqil) of the division from someone else without endorsing it, or if he is committed to its correctness (multazim li-sihhatihi). This determines the objector's first move.
2
Check for transmission challenges
If the presenter is a transmitter not committed to correctness, the objector can only demand proof of correct transmission. Once proven, the debate ends on that point.
The presenter proves transmission using standard methods like those for hadith.
3
If presenter is committed, examine wording
If the presenter authored the division or commits to it, the objector examines the wording for ambiguity (ghamid), obscurity (mubham), or misleading expressions (mu'him li-ma'na ghayr sahih). If found, the objector requests clarification.
The presenter must then clarify his intended meaning.
4
If wording is clear, move to essence of division
Once clarity is established, the objector examines the essence (jawhar) of the division: its type, the relation (nisba) between the divided whole (muqsam) and its parts (aqsam), and the relations among the parts themselves.
This step is only reached after source and clarity are satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترتيب المناظرة في التقسيم تتبع الخطوات التاليات لدى المناظرة في التقسيم : أولاً : ينظر في التقسيم، فإذا كان مورده ناقلاً له عن غيره، غير ملتزم لصحته، فليس للمعترض (المستدل) إلا أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل، ومتى أثبت مورد التقسيم ما يفيد أن نقله صحيح، وفق طرائق إثبات النقول والمرويات، فقد أدى ما عليه، وينتهي الأمر بذلك. أما إذا جاء بالتقسيم من عنده، أو ذكره دون أن ينقله عن غيره، أو كان ناقلاً له وأعلن التزامه بصحته، فإن مناقشته والاعتراض عليه تكون وفق ما يلي : ثانياً : ينظر في التقسيم، هل فيه لفظ غامض، أو مبهم الدلالة، أو موهم لمعنى غير صحيح، أو لا؟ فإن كان فيه شيء من ذلك كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يستفسر، أي : أن يطلب تفسير الغامض أو بيان المبهم، أو بيان المراد من اللفظ الذي يوهم ما لا يصح، وعلى مورد التقسيم أن يبين مراده . وإذا لم يكن في التقسيم شيء من ذلك فإلى الخطوة التالية : ثالثاً : ثم ينظر في جوهر التقسيم، وذلك بالتعرف على نوعه، وتبين النسبة بين المقسم والأقسام كلها، وتبين النسبة بين كل قسم وبين ما عداه من
The arrangement of disputation concerning division: Follow the following steps in disputation concerning division: First: One examines the division. If its presenter is transmitting it from someone else, not committed to its correctness, then the objector has only to demand proof of the correctness of the transmission. And when the presenter of the division establishes what indicates that his transmission is correct, according to the methods of establishing transmissions and narrations, he has fulfilled his obligation, and the matter ends there. But if he presents the division from himself, or mentions it without transmitting it from someone else, or is transmitting it but declares his commitment to its correctness, then its discussion and objection are according to what follows: Second: One examines the division: does it contain an ambiguous expression, or one obscure in signification, or one suggesting an incorrect meaning, or not? If it contains any of that, then the objector may inquire, i.e., request an explanation of the ambiguous, or clarification of the obscure, or clarification of the intended meaning of the expression that suggests what is not correct, and the presenter of the division must clarify his intended meaning. And if the division does not contain any of that, then on to the next step: Third: Then one examines the essence of the division, by identifying its type, and clarifying the relation between the divided whole and all the parts, and clarifying the relation between each part and what is other than it from...
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage outlines the procedural steps for disputing a division in jadal. The author presents three ordered steps. The first step concerns the source of the division: if the presenter is merely transmitting a division from someone else without committing to its correctness, the objector can only challenge the accuracy of the transmission. Once the presenter proves the transmission is correct according to standard methods (like those used for hadith), the debate ends. However, if the presenter himself authored the division or commits to its correctness, the debate proceeds. The second step checks for unclear wording: ambiguous (ghamid), obscure (mubham), or misleading (muhim li-ma'na ghayr sahih) expressions. If found, the objector may request clarification, and the presenter must explain. The third step, partially given, then examines the essence of the division: its type (e.g., binary or multi-part), the relation between the divided whole (muqsam) and its parts (aqsam), and the mutual relations among the parts. This structured approach ensures that objections are raised at appropriate levels, preventing premature attacks on the division's logical structure.

Key Terms
مورد التقسيم (muwarrid al-taqsim): The presenter of the division; the one who introduces the division in the debate.
ناقل (naqil): A transmitter who reports a division from another person, not necessarily endorsing it.
ملتزم لصحته (multazim li-sihhatihi): Committed to the correctness of the division; the presenter declares his own belief in its validity.
جوهر التقسيم (jawhar al-taqsim): The essence of the division; its intrinsic logical structure, including type and relations.
النسبة (al-nisba): The relation; between the divided whole and its parts, and between each part and the others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the procedural steps, what should the objector do if the division's presenter is transmitting it from another without committing to its correctness?
A) Challenge the division directly on its content
B) Demand proof of correct transmission
C) Inquire about ambiguous wording
D) Reject the division outright

Key Takeaway
The three-step procedure ensures that objections to a division are raised at the appropriate level: source, then clarity, then essence.

The Order of Debate in Division
Procedure for debating a logical division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the three-step procedure for debating a logical division (taqsim) and identify the correct responses for both the objector and the proponent according to the rules of Islamic dialectic.

Core Idea
Debating a division requires checking its source, clarity, and structure before accepting or challenging it.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every division is accepted at face value. This passage from the Dawabit al-Ma'rifa lays out a precise protocol for debating a taqsim. Understanding this procedure is essential for engaging in rigorous theological and juristic discussions that rely on proper classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's stance on the division's origin
Determine whether the proponent (muwarrid al-taqsim) transmits the division from someone else without committing to its correctness, or presents it as his own or commits to it. This determines the objector's initial options.
If transmitted without commitment, only transmission needs proof; otherwise full procedure applies.
2
Check the division's language for ambiguity
Examine the division for any ambiguous, obscure, or misleading expressions. The objector has the right to demand clarification, and the proponent must respond.
This step precedes any judgment on the division's logical structure.
3
Examine the division's logical structure
Identify the type of division (whole into parts or universal into particulars). Verify the relationships between the divided term (muqsam) and the parts (aqsam), and between the parts themselves. Check the conditions: exhaustiveness, mutual exclusivity, and appropriate specificity hierarchy.
If conditions are met, the division must be accepted; otherwise the objector can point out the flaw.
4
Determine the objector's options
Based on the examination, the objector (mu'tarid/mustadill) can either accept the division (if conditions hold) or object by detailing the flaw and providing evidence. He may also declare the division invalid.
The objector may also bring up unaccepted claims, shifting the debate to those.
5
Determine the proponent's responses
The proponent has two main options: accept the objection and correct the division, or reject the objection by clarifying his intended meaning of the divided term, parts, division, or underlying doctrine. He may give multiple answers. If the objector raises new claims, the debate turns to those.
This step shows the dialectical flexibility within the procedural framework.

ترتيب المناظرة في التقسيم
تتبع الخطوات التاليات لدى المناظرة في التقسيم :
أولاً : ينظر في التقسيم، فإذا كان مورده ناقلاً له عن غيره، غير ملتزم لصحته، فليس للمعترض (المستدل) إلا أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل، ومتى أثبت مورد التقسيم ما يفيد أن نقله صحيح، وفق طرائق إثبات النقول والمرويات، فقد أدى ما عليه، وينتهي الأمر بذلك. أما إذا جاء بالتقسيم من عنده، أو ذكره دون أن ينقله عن غيره، أو كان ناقلاً له وأعلن التزامه بصحته، فإن مناقشته والاعتراض عليه تكون وفق ما يلي :
ثانياً : ينظر في التقسيم، هل فيه لفظ غامض، أو مبهم الدلالة، أو موهم لمعنى غير صحيح، أو لا؟ فإن كان فيه شيء من ذلك كان للمعترض (المستدل) أن يستفسر، أي : أن يطلب تفسير الغامض أو بيان المبهم، أو بيان المراد من اللفظ الذي يوهم ما لا يصح، وعلى مورد التقسيم أن يبين مراده .
وإذا لم يكن في التقسيم شيء من ذلك فإلى الخطوة التالية :
ثالثاً : ثم ينظر في جوهر التقسيم، وذلك بالتعرف على نوعه، وتبين النسبة بين المقسم والأقسام كلها، وتبين النسبة بين كل قسم وبين ما عداه من الأقسام، فإذا كان التقسيم مستوفياً شروطه(١) التي سبق بيانها، وجب التسليم بصحته، وإذا كان فيه إخلال بشرط من الشروط، أو لا ينطبق على نوعه، كان للمعترض أن يوجه اعتراضه، متضمناً بيان الخلل الذي رآه في التقسيم، ومتضمناً الدليل على ذلك، وكان له أن يُعلن فساد التقسيم، بناء على ما فيه من خلل .
وموقف صاحب التقسيم أمام اعتراض (المستدل) يتلخص بواحد من أمرين :
الأول : أن يعلن موافقته على الاعتراض ويُسَلّم به، ويعدل إلى تقسيم آخر، أو يصحح تقسيمه .
الثاني : أن يمنع الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من المَقْسِم، أو الأقسام، أو التقسيم، أو المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه التقسيم .
وللمانع صاحب التقسيم أن يجيب على اعتراض المستدل بأكثر من جواب، محرراً مراده من المقسم، ومن الأقسام، ومن التقسيم، ومن المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه تقسيمه .
وقد يكون كلام المعترض مشتملاً على دعاوى غير مسلمة، فلصاحب التقسيم عندئذ أن يمنعها، وتتحول المناظرة حينئذ إلى المناظرة في التصديقات، حول دعاوى جديدة أوردها المعترض (المستدل).
(١) تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه يشترط فيه أن يكون حاصراً، أي : جامعاً مانعاً، وأن يكون كل قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام، ومبايناً للمقسم فلا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. وتقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته يشترط فيه أن يكون حاصراً، أي : جامعاً مانعاً، وأن يكون كل قسم أخص مطلقاً من المقسم، وأن يكون كل قسم مبايناً لما عداه من الأقسام .
The order of the debate concerning division follows the subsequent steps: First: The division is examined. If its proponent transmits it from someone else, not committing to its correctness, then the objector (arguer) may only demand proof of the correctness of the transmission. Whenever the proponent of the division demonstrates that his transmission is correct, according to the methods of verifying transmitted reports, he has fulfilled his duty, and the matter ends there. However, if he presents the division from himself, or mentions it without transmitting it from someone else, or transmits it but declares his commitment to its correctness, then its discussion and objection proceed as follows: Second: The division is examined: is there in it an ambiguous expression, an obscure indication, or one suggesting an incorrect meaning, or not? If there is any such thing, the objector (arguer) may inquire, i.e., demand an explanation of the ambiguous, clarification of the obscure, or clarification of the intended meaning of the expression that suggests what is incorrect. The proponent of the division must clarify his intent. If there is nothing of this sort in the division, then to the next step: Third: Then the essence of the division is examined by identifying its type, clarifying the relationship between the divided term and all the parts, and clarifying the relationship between each part and the other parts. If the division fulfills its conditions (1) that were previously explained, one must acknowledge its correctness. If it violates one of the conditions, or does not apply to its type, the objector may direct his objection, including an explanation of the flaw he sees in the division and including evidence for that, and he may declare the division invalid based on the flaw in it. The position of the proponent of the division facing the objection of the arguer is summarized in one of two matters: First: He announces his agreement with the objection and accepts it, and changes to another division or corrects his division. Second: He prevents the objection by clarifying his intent regarding the divided term, or the parts, or the division, or the scholarly doctrine upon which he based the division. The preventing proponent of the division may respond to the arguer's objection with more than one answer, clarifying his intent regarding the divided term, the parts, the division, and the scholarly doctrine upon which he based his division. The objector's speech may contain unaccepted claims; then the proponent of the division may prevent them, and the debate then turns into a debate about assents (tasdiqat) concerning new claims brought by the objector (arguer). (1) The division of a whole into its parts requires that it be exhaustive, i.e., comprehensive and exclusive, and that each part be distinct from the other parts, and distinct from the divided term so that neither is predicated of the other. The division of a universal into its particulars requires that it be exhaustive, i.e., comprehensive and exclusive, and that each part be absolutely more specific than the divided term, and that each part be distinct from the other parts.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage outlines the procedural steps for debating a division (taqsim) in a dialectical context. It is a methodological guide addressing the roles of the proponent (muwarrid) and the objector (mu'tarid/ mustadill). The author first distinguishes two scenarios regarding the source of the division: if the proponent merely transmits it from another without committing to its correctness, the objector can only question the transmission's authenticity. If the proponent presents it as his own or commits to its correctness, then the full debate procedure applies. The procedure then follows three steps: Step one examines the division's origin and the proponent's commitment; step two investigates the clarity of the language—whether ambiguous, obscure, or misleading—and grants the objector the right to seek clarification; step three examines the essence of the division by identifying its type (whole-to-parts or universal-to-particulars) and checking the relationships between the divided term and parts, and between the parts themselves. If the division meets the conditions (stated in the footnote: exhaustiveness, mutual exclusivity, and specific hierarchy), it must be accepted; otherwise, the objector may point out the flaw and provide evidence. After an objection, the proponent has two options: accept the objection and amend the division, or reject the objection by clarifying his intended meaning of the divided term, parts, division, or underlying doctrine. The proponent may give multiple answers. If the objector brings unaccepted claims, the debate shifts to a discussion of tasdiqat (assents) about those new claims. The footnote specifies conditions for two types of division: division of a whole into parts (taqsim al-kull ila ajza'ihi) requires exhaustiveness (jam' wa man') and mutual exclusivity (mubayana) of parts, and also that parts are distinct from the whole such that neither is predicated of the other. Division of a universal into particulars requires exhaustiveness, that each part is absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) than the universal, and that parts are mutually exclusive. This passage is essential for understanding the rules of debate about definitions and classifications in Islamic logic and dialectic.

Key Terms
المناظرة
التقسيم
المعترض (المستدل)
مورد التقسيم
حاصر

C) First check for ambiguous terms, then proceed to essence.
D) Accept the division without debate.

Key Takeaway
The integrity of a logical division in Islamic debate depends on a rigorous three-step examination: source, clarity, and structure.

Debate Roles: Sa'il and Mu'allil
Author introduces terminology for debate roles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of sa'il and mu'allil in a munazara and explain how the roles can reverse when a new assent is presented.

Core Idea
Debate roles are dynamic: the defender can become the objector.

When you enter a scholarly debate, you need to know who is defending and who is objecting. This passage from the rules of debate on assents defines these roles precisely and explains that they are not fixed: the same person can switch roles depending on the flow of argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two roles
Find the terms 'sa'il' (questioner) and 'mu'allil' (answerer). The passage defines the sa'il as the one who directs an objection against an assent, and the mu'allil as the one who defends it.
2
Note the default order
The mu'allil is originally the one who begins speaking by presenting an assent. The sa'il then objects.
3
Identify the condition for reversal
The passage states that when the sa'il presents a new assent, he becomes the mu'allil, and his counterpart becomes the sa'il.
4
Apply to a kalam debate
Imagine a debate on the temporal origination of the world. The mu'allil claims 'The world is temporally originated'. The sa'il objects. If the sa'il then claims 'Eternal existence is impossible for contingent beings', he becomes the mu'allil for that new claim.

ويكون (المعلِّل) في الأصل هو البادىء بالكلام، أو مُقدِّم التصديق جواباً لسؤال، ثم يوجه (السائل) عليه اعتراضه، ولكن قد ينعكس الأمر ضمن المناظرة، وذلك إذ يتحول (السائل) إلى مُقدِّم تصديق جديد، فيكون حينئذٍ هو (المعلِّل)، ويكون مقابله الذي يعترض عليه هو (السائل)، وهكذا يتحول الأمر كلما تحوّل (السائل) إلى مقدم تصديق جديد.

ولما كان قسم البديهي الخفي مما قد يغفل الفكر عن كونه بديهياً، كان الغافل عن بداهته بحاجة إلى ما ينبهه على حقيقته، فللمعترض حينئذٍ أن
Rules of debate on assents:
A) We have previously known that the one who directs an objection against an assent is called a 'questioner' (sa'il), and the owner of the assent or the one who appoints himself to defend it and to show the invalidity of the objection is called an 'answerer' (mu'allil).
The answerer is originally the one who begins speaking, or the one who presents an assent in answer to a question; then the questioner directs his objection against it. But the matter may reverse within the debate, when the questioner becomes the presenter of a new assent, so then he becomes the answerer, and his counterpart who objects to him becomes the questioner. And so the matter switches every time the questioner turns into a presenter of a new assent.
B) What the debate on assents concerns:
Since the utmost effort of the arguer (mustadill) for a claim he presents is to return to the clear self-evidents (badihiyyat jaliyya) and rely upon them, and build upon them a correct structure that yields the correctness of his claim; and since the clear self-evidents are the first thing in the ladder of intellectual knowledges, and nothing is clearer or more apparent in thought than them, such that one refers to them in proving them if a denier denies them or an objector objects to them; since this is the case, these clear self-evidents are not subjects for debate at all.
And since the category of hidden self-evident (badihi khafi) is such that thought may neglect its being self-evident, the one who is heedless of its self-evidence needs something to alert him to its reality. Therefore the objector may then...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage introduces the rules for debate (munazara) concerning assents (tasdiqat). It defines two key roles: the questioner (sa'il) who raises objections, and the answerer (mu'allil) who defends a proposition. The answerer is originally the first speaker, but roles can flip when the questioner presents a new assent. Then the author discusses the scope of debate: clear self-evident propositions (badihiyyat jaliyya) are not subject to debate because they are the foundational reference point. However, hidden self-evident propositions (badihi khafi) can be debated because someone may be unaware of their self-evidence, and the objector can point this out. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the idea is clear. This is a dialectical passage, laying out procedural rules for logical debate.
