Understanding Sanad in Jadal through Examples
Author gives sanad examples and classifies sanad by relation to claim's negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze a 'man' maqrunan bil-sanad' (prevention with support) in a jadal exchange from Islamic legal or theological debate.

Core Idea
In jadal, a good objection must be supported by a reason that pinpoints the opponent's error.

In this lesson, you will examine two concrete examples of how a dialectician objects to a claim by accompanying his prevention (man') with a supporting reason (sanad). These examples, drawn from fiqh and astronomy, illustrate the practical application of sanad before we delve into its formal classification. Pay close attention to how the sanad shifts the burden of proof back to the proponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
In the first example, what is the jurist's claim? In the second, what is the claim about Mars? Write down the exact proposition.
The claims are: 'Water does not become impure by mere contact with impurity' and 'Mars is a star.'
2
Identify the objection
The objector says 'mamnu'' (prevented) and then provides a reason. What is the reason in each case?
For water: 'what you mentioned is specific to quantities above two qullas.' For Mars: 'if Mars were a glowing celestial body.'
3
Determine the type of sanad
The author calls the first a 'sanad hilli' (support by legal cause) and the second a 'hall' (solution). What do these terms indicate about the nature of the support?
Sanad hilli points to a legal distinction; hall indicates a condition that must be met.
4
Analyze the effect
How does the sanad shift the burden of proof back to the proponent? Why is the proponent now required to provide evidence?
The objector shows that the claim is not universally true or depends on an unproven condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة :
أ ـ قال الفقيه صاحب التصديق : الماء لا ينجس بمجرد ملاقاة النجاسة له .
فاعترض (السائل) عليه بالمنع مقروناً بالسند الحلّي، فقال له : هذه الدعوى غير مسلمة، وما ذكرت خاص فيما زاد على القلتين، أي : إني ألفت نظرك إلى ما أرى أنه منشأ غلطك، فما دليل صحة دعواك؟ .
ب ـ قال صاحب التصديق : المريخ نجم .
فاعترض (السائل) عليه بالمنع مقروناً بالحلّ، فقال له : لا أسلم أن المريخ نجم، وما ذكرت يصح لو كان المريخ جرماً سماوياً ملتهباً، أي : أطالبك بالدليل على دعواك، وألفت نظرك إلى ما أرى أنه منشأ غلطك .
هذه أقسام السند باعتبار الصورة التي يُورد عليها .
وينقسم السند أيضاً إلى أقسام أخرى باعتبار آخر، هو اعتبار نسبته إلى نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، فهو ينقسم بهذا الاعتبار الآخر إلى ستة أقسام اقتضاها الاحتمال العقلي، وهي كما يلي :
الأول : ما يكون نفس نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا ينفع السائل الإتيان به، وينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بالرد عليه .
مثاله من اللُّمّي :
المعلّل : العالم قديم .
السائل : ممنوع، لم لا يكون غير قديم؟
مثاله من القطعي :
المعلّل : الإنسان متطور من حيوان دونه .
السائل : ممنوع، كيف والإنسان ليس متطوراً من حيوان دونه .
الثاني : ما يكون مساوياً لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا ينفع السائل الإتيان به، وينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بالرد عليه .
مثاله من اللمي:
المعلّل: العالم قديم.
السائل: ممنوع، لم لا يكون حادثاً؟ (فكونه حادثاً هو مساوي النقيض).
مثاله من القطعي:
المعلل: الإنسان متطور من حيوان دونه.
السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو مخلوق بالخلق المباشر من الطين؟ (فهذا مساوي النقيض).
الثالث: ما يكون أعمّ مطلقاً من نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به، ولكنه لو خالف وجاء به أفاد المعلّل إبطاله، لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص.
مثاله من اللمي:
المعلّل: العنقاء طائر موجود فعلاً.
السائل: ممنوع، لم لا يكون معلوماً؟.
فكونه معلوماً أعم مطلقاً من نقيض (هو موجود)، لأن نقيض (هو موجود) هو غير موجود، والمعلوم أعم مطلقاً من (غير موجود).
مثاله من القطعي:
المعلّل: الخفاش ليس بطائر.
السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو حيوان؟.
فكونه حيواناً أعم مطلقاً من (هو طائر) الذي هو نقيض الدعوى (ليس بطائر).
المعلّل: المؤمن لا يعذب.
السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو يجازى؟ (فهذا أعم مطلقاً من نقيض الدعوى).
الرابع: ما يكون أخصّ مطلقاً من نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا ينفع
Examples:
A. The jurist, the proponent, said: Water does not become impure merely by coming into contact with impurity. The objector objected with prevention accompanied by a halal support, saying: This claim is not conceded, and what you mentioned is specific to amounts exceeding two qullas, i.e., I draw your attention to what I consider the source of your error. So what is the proof of the validity of your claim?
B. The proponent said: Mars is a star. The objector objected with prevention accompanied by a solution, saying: I do not concede that Mars is a star, and what you mentioned would be correct if Mars were a glowing celestial body, i.e., I demand proof for your claim, and I draw your attention to what I consider the source of your error.
These are the divisions of sanad according to the form in which it is presented.
Sanad is also divided into other divisions according to another consideration: consideration of its relation to the negation of the prevented claim. So it is divided by this other consideration into six divisions required by rational possibility, and they are as follows:
First: That which is exactly the negation of the prevented claim. This benefits the objector to bring it, and benefits the respondent to engage in refuting it.
Example from lammiy: The respondent: The world is eternal. The objector: Prevented, why is it not non-eternal?
Example from qat'i: The respondent: Man evolved from an inferior animal. The objector: Prevented, how is it while man did not evolve from an inferior animal?
Second: That which is equivalent to the negation of the prevented claim. This benefits the objector to bring it, and benefits the respondent to engage in refuting it.
Example from lammiy: The respondent: The world is eternal. The objector: Prevented, why is it not originated? (Its being originated is equivalent to the negation).
Example from qat'i: The respondent: Man evolved from an inferior animal. The objector: Prevented, how is it while he is directly created from clay? (This is equivalent to the negation).
Third: That which is absolutely more general than the negation of the prevented claim. It is not permissible for the objector to bring it, but if he violates and brings it, it benefits the respondent to invalidate it, because the negation of the more general necessitates the negation of the more specific.
Example from lammiy: The respondent: The phoenix is a bird existing actually. The objector: Prevented, why is it not known? For being known is absolutely more general than the negation of 'it exists', because the negation of 'it exists' is 'it does not exist', and the known is absolutely more general than 'does not exist'.
Example from qat'i: The respondent: The bat is not a bird. The objector: Prevented, how is it while it is an animal? For being animal is absolutely more general than 'it is a bird' which is the negation of the claim 'it is not a bird'.
The respondent: The believer will not be punished. The objector: Prevented, how is it while he is requited? (This is absolutely more general than the negation of the claim).
Fourth: That which is absolutely more specific than the negation of the prevented claim, and this benefits
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of sanad (support) in the context of jadal (dialectical exchange). It begins with two illustrative examples of 'man' maqrunan bil-sanad' (prevention accompanied by support). In the first example, a jurist claims water does not become impure by mere contact with impurity; the objector replies with a 'man' maqrunan bil-sanad al-hilli' (prevention with a legal cause support), pointing out that the claim only applies to quantities above two qullas. The second example involves the claim that Mars is a star; the objector responds with prevention and a solution, demanding proof. These examples demonstrate a previous classification of sanad by its form (surah).

The author then introduces another classification of sanad based on its relation to the negation (naqid) of the prevented claim. This classification yields six divisions, of which the first four are detailed here. The first division is when the sanad is exactly the negation of the claim; the objector may bring it, and the respondent must refute it. Examples from both lammiy (probabilistic) and qat'i (certain) reasoning are given. The second division is when the sanad is equivalent (musawi) to the negation; it also benefits both parties. The third division is when the sanad is absolutely more general (a'amm mutlaq) than the negation; the objector should not use it because negating the general implies negating the specific, aiding the respondent. Examples include the phoenix and bat cases. The fourth division is when the sanad is more specific; the text begins this division but the example is cut off.

Key Terms
السند
المنع مقروناً بالسند
الحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objector do in the example of Mars?
A) He denies the claim without reason.
B) He provides a support showing the claim is conditional on Mars being a glowing celestial body.
C) He accepts the claim.
D) He changes the subject.

Key Takeaway
A proper objection in jadal is not a flat denial; it is a 'man' maqrunan bil-sanad' that identifies a flaw in the claim and demands proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a debate about whether the world is eternal, the objector says: 'Mamnu', why is it not originated?' Is this a man' maqrunan bil-sanad? Why or why not? If not, how would you turn it into one?

Take the claim 'The Quran is created.' Formulate a man' maqrunan bil-sanad that a mutakallim might use, identifying the sanad and how it challenges the claim.

Classifying Sanad by Relation to the Negation of the Claim
Author gives sanad examples and classifies sanad by relation to claim's negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sanad in a jadal exchange into one of six types based on its logical relation to the negation of the claim, and evaluate its strategic value for the objector and respondent.

Core Idea
The effectiveness of a sanad depends on whether it is exactly the negation, equivalent, more general, or more specific relative to the claim's negation.

The author now classifies sanad according to its relation to naqid al-da'wa (the negation of the claim). This classification, based on rational possibility, helps the dialectician choose the most effective support and anticipate the opponent's refutation. You will learn the first four types, each illustrated with examples from both lammiy (probabilistic) and qat'i (certain) reasoning. Note that the fourth type is incomplete in the source text.

Guided Path
1
Understand naqid al-da'wa
The negation of the claim is the direct contradictory. For 'The world is eternal,' its naqid is 'The world is not eternal.' For 'Man evolved from an inferior animal,' the naqid is 'Man did not evolve from an inferior animal.' Identify these in each example.
The naqid is the opposite proposition that would be true if the claim is false.
2
Extract the logical relation for each type
For each of the first three types, determine whether the sanad is exactly the negation (type 1), equivalent to it (type 2), or absolutely more general (type 3). The examples make these relations clear.
Type 1: sanad = naqid; Type 2: sanad = equivalent (e.g., 'originated' is equivalent to 'not eternal'); Type 3: sanad is broader (e.g., 'known' is broader than 'does not exist').
3
Analyze strategic value
For each type, determine who benefits. The author states that types 1 and 2 benefit both the objector and respondent. Type 3 is not permissible for the objector because it helps the respondent. Why?
Because negating the general (e.g., 'not known') implies negating the specific (e.g., 'does not exist'), making it easy for the respondent to refute.
4
Apply to new examples
Given a claim and a sanad, classify it into one of the three types. For instance, claim: 'The believer will not be punished.' Sanad: 'Why is he requited?' Is this type 1, 2, or 3?
The text gives this as an example of type 3: 'requited' is more general than 'not punished' (negation).

First Type of Dialectical Objection: Equal to the Negation (Musawi al-Naqid)
Defines and exemplifies the first type of objection equal to the negation of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection that is equal to the negation of a claim and distinguish it from other types of objections.

Core Idea
An objection equal to the negation simply states the opposite of the claim.

In dialectical debates (jadal), the objector (sa'il) challenges the claimant (mu'allil) by saying 'mamnu'' and then providing a reason. The first and simplest type of objection is when the proposition put forward by the objector is exactly equivalent to the negation (naqid) of the claimant's claim. This passage gives two clear examples from different types of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
In the passage, who is the mu'allil (claimant) and who is the sa'il (objector) in each example? The claimant makes a statement; the objector says 'mamnu'' and then a question.
The terms are explicitly used in the Arabic.
2
Extract the claim (mudda'a)
What proposition is the claimant putting forward in each example? Write down the exact claim.
First example: 'al-ʿālamu qadīm' (the world is eternal). Second: 'al-insānu mutaṭawwirun min ḥayawān dūnah' (man evolved from a lower animal).
3
Determine the negation (naqid)
What is the logical contradictory of that claim? The negation should be a proposition that cannot be true at the same time as the claim.
Negation of 'world is eternal' is 'world is not eternal' or equivalently 'world is originated'. Negation of 'man evolved from a lower animal' is 'man did not evolve from a lower animal'.
4
Compare the objector's proposition
Look at what the objector says: 'why not originated?' and 'how while created directly from clay?'. Does that proposition match the negation exactly?
In both cases, the objector's implied proposition is exactly the negation of the claim.
5
Recognize the pattern
In musawi al-naqid, the objector's proposition is exactly equal to the negation. There is no additional content or specification; it is a direct opposite. This forces the claimant to provide evidence for the original claim.
This is the simplest type of objection; it does not require the objector to produce a counter-argument, just the negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثاله من اللمي: المعلّل: العالم قديم. السائل: ممنوع، لم لا يكون حادثاً؟ (فكونه حادثاً هو مساوي النقيض). مثاله من القطعي: المعلل: الإنسان متطور من حيوان دونه. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو مخلوق بالخلق المباشر من الطين؟ (فهذا مساوي النقيض).
An example from lam'i reasoning: The claimant: The world is eternal. The objector: Prevented, why not be originated? (So being originated is equal to the negation). An example from qat'i reasoning: The claimant: Man evolved from a lower animal. The objector: Prevented, how while he is created by direct creation from clay? (So this is equal to the negation).
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of objection (man') in dialectical debate. The objector's proposition is exactly equal to the negation (naqid) of the claimant's proposition. In the first example (lam'i/causal reasoning), the claim is 'the world is eternal'; its negation is 'the world is originated'. The objector says 'why not originated?' which is exactly the negation. In the second example (qat'i/certain reasoning), the claim is 'man evolved from a lower animal'; its negation is 'man did not evolve from a lower animal'. The objector says 'how while he is created directly from clay?' which implies the negation. This type is straightforward: the objector simply states the opposite of the claim.

نقيض

Key Takeaway
Musawi al-naqid is the simplest objection: the objector merely says the opposite of the claim, forcing the claimant to provide evidence.

Third Type: Objections More General Absolutely than the Negation
Defines and exemplifies objections that are absolutely more general than the negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection is absolutely more general than the negation of the claim, and explain why such objections are forbidden for the objector.

Core Idea
An objection more general than the negation of the claim self-destructs because denying the general denies the specific.

In dialectical debate, not all objections are permitted. The third type of objection is one that is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaqan) than the negation of the claim. This lesson explains why such objections are invalid for the objector and how the claimant can exploit them. We will learn the rule 'nafy al-a'am yastalzim nafy al-akhass' and see examples from lam'i and qat'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its negation
Find the claimant's proposition (da'wa) and its logical opposite (naqid). For example, in the phoenix example, the claim is 'The phoenix is an actual existent.' Its negation is 'The phoenix is non-existent.'
2
Identify the objector's proposition
The objector says something like 'Why not be known?' implying 'It is known.' This is the proposition the objector wants to use to challenge the claim.
3
Check if it is absolutely more general than the negation
Determine whether every instance of the negation falls under the objector's proposition, but not vice versa. Here, 'non-existent' is a subset of 'known' (everything non-existent is known as an idea, but not everything known is non-existent). So 'known' is absolutely more general.
Use the definition: A is absolutely more general than B if all B are A, but not all A are B.
4
Apply the principle nafy al-a'am yastalzim nafy al-akhass
If the more general is negated, the more specific is also negated. So if the claimant can deny the objector's proposition, that automatically denies the negation of the claim.
This principle makes the objection harmful to the objector.
5
Conclude why the objection is invalid for the objector
The objector should not bring such an objection because it gives the claimant an easy path to refute the negation of his claim, thereby strengthening his own position.
The text says 'it is not permissible for the objector to bring it'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: ما يكون أعمّ مطلقاً من نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به، ولكنه لو خالف وجاء به أفاد المعلّل إبطاله، لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص. مثاله من اللمي: المعلّل: العنقاء طائر موجود فعلاً. السائل: ممنوع، لم لا يكون معلوماً؟. فكونه معلوماً أعم مطلقاً من نقيض (هو موجود)، لأن نقيض (هو موجود) هو غير موجود، والمعلوم أعم مطلقاً من (غير موجود). مثاله من القطعي: المعلّل: الخفاش ليس بطائر. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو حيوان؟. فكونه حيواناً أعم مطلقاً من (هو طائر) الذي هو نقيض الدعوى (ليس بطائر). المعلّل: المؤمن لا يعذب. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو يجازى؟ (فهذا أعم مطلقاً من نقيض الدعوى).
Third: What is absolutely more general than the negation of the prevented claim. It is not permissible for the objector to bring it, but if he violates and brings it, it benefits the claimant to invalidate it, because the negation of the more general entails the negation of the more specific. An example from lam'i: The claimant: The phoenix is a bird existing actually. The objector: Prevented, why not be known? So its being known is absolutely more general than the negation of (it is existent), because the negation of (it is existent) is non-existent, and the known is absolutely more general than (non-existent). An example from qat'i: The claimant: The bat is not a bird. The objector: Prevented, how while it is an animal? So its being an animal is absolutely more general than (it is a bird) which is the negation of the claim (not a bird). Also: The claimant: The believer is not punished. The objector: Prevented, how while he is recompensed? (So this is absolutely more general than the negation of the claim).
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of objection, where the objector's proposition is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaqan) than the negation of the claim. The rule is that the objector should not use such an objection, but if he does, the claimant can easily refute it because denying the more general necessarily denies the more specific (since if the general is negated, the specific under it is also negated). The lam'i example: The claimant says 'the phoenix actually exists'; its negation is 'the phoenix does not exist'. The objector says 'why not be known?', and 'known' is broader than 'non-existent' (since not everything known is non-existent, but non-existent is a subset of known? Actually the text says 'known' is a'am mutlaqan from 'non-existent' because everything non-existent is known (as an idea), but not everything known is non-existent. So if the claimant can show that the objector's proposition (known) is negated, then the more specific (non-existent) is also negated, which would support the negation of the claim. The qat'i examples: (1) Claim: 'Bat is not a bird.' Negation: 'it is a bird.' Objector: 'it is an animal.' Animal is more general than bird. (2) Claim: 'Believer is not punished.' Negation: 'believer is punished.' Objector: 'he is recompensed.' Recompensed is more general than punished (since recompense includes reward and punishment).

Key Terms
أعم مطلقاً
نقيض الدعوى
نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because 'animal' is completely different from 'bird'.
C) Because 'animal' is more general than the negation 'bird', so the claimant can negate 'animal' to negate 'bird'.

Key Takeaway
An objection that is absolutely more general than the negation of the claim is invalid because the claimant can simply negate the more general to negate the more specific, turning the objection against the objector.

Fifth Type: Objection with Partial Overlap
Defines and exemplifies objections that have partial overlap (wajhi) with the negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection has partial overlap with the negation of the claim and explain why it is invalid in jadal.

Core Idea
Partial overlap is not enough; only full inclusion challenges a claim.

In dialectic, the objector must directly challenge the claim or its basis. This lesson introduces a specific type of invalid objection: one where the objector's proposition only partially overlaps with the negation of the claim. Such objections are irrelevant and do not need a response.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim and its negation
In each example, identify the claimant's statement (mudda'a) and what its direct negation would be. For instance, 'The idol-worshipper is an infidel' – its negation is 'The idol-worshipper is not an infidel'.
2
Identify the objector's proposition
What does the objector bring up as an alternative? In the first example, 'rich'; in the second, 'human'. Note the exact term used.
3
Analyze the relation between the objector's proposition and the negation
Determine whether the objector's class (e.g., 'rich') is fully included in the negation class ('not infidel'), fully excluded, or partially overlapping. Use the logical relation of 'umum wa khusus wajhi' – partial overlap.
Partial overlap means the two classes intersect but neither contains the other.
4
Apply the rule
If the relation is partial overlap (wajhi), the objection is not permissible (la yajuz). The claimant should ignore it and not waste time refuting it (la yanfa' al-ishtighal bi-ibtalihi).
This rule prevents dialectical dead ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: ما يكون بينه وبين الدعوى الممنوعة عموم وخصوص وجهي، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به والاستناد إليه، ولا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بإبطاله. مثاله من اللُّمّي: المعلّل: الذي يعبد الأوثان كافر. السائل: ممنوع، لمَ لا يكون غنياً؟ (فبين الغني والكافر عموم وخصوص وجهي). مثاله من القطعي: المعلّل: من أعلن الشهادتين فهو داخل في زمرة المسلمين. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو إنسان؟ (فبين الإنسان وبين من هو داخل في زمرة المسلمين عموم وخصوص وجهي).
Fifth: What is between it and the prevented claim partial generality and specificity (wajhi). It is not permissible for the objector to bring it and rely on it, and it does not benefit the claimant to engage in invalidating it. An example from lam'i: The claimant: He who worships idols is an infidel. The objector: Prevented, why not be rich? (So between rich and infidel there is partial generality and specificity). An example from qat'i: The claimant: He who proclaims the two shahadas is within the fold of Muslims. The objector: Prevented, how while he is a human? (So between human and he who is within the fold of Muslims there is partial generality and specificity).
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth type of objection, where the objector's proposition has a partial overlap (wajhi) with the negation of the claim. This means the two classes intersect but neither fully contains the other. Such an objection is not permissible for the objector, and the claimant has no need to refute it because it does not directly challenge the claim. The lam'i example: The claim is 'idol-worshipper is an infidel'; its negation is 'idol-worshipper is not an infidel' or more directly 'not an infidel'. The objector says 'why not rich?' The class of 'rich' and 'infidel' overlap partially (some rich are infidels, some are not; some infidels are rich, some are poor). This does not negate the claim. The qat'i example: Claim: 'One who proclaims shahadas is a Muslim.' Negation: 'not a Muslim.' Objector says 'how while he is a human?' Human and Muslim have partial overlap (all Muslims are human, but not all humans are Muslim). Again, this does not invalidate the claim.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص وجهي
لا يجوز للسائل
لا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بإبطاله
غني
إنسان

B) Because 'rich' is more specific than 'infidel'.
C) Because 'rich' and 'infidel' have only partial overlap, so it does not directly contradict the claim.

Key Takeaway
An objection that only partially overlaps with the negation of the claim is invalid and the claimant should ignore it.

Sixth Type of Objection: The Irrelevant (Mubayin) Objection
Defines and partially exemplifies objections completely different (mubayin) from the negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection that is completely different (mubayin) from the negation of the claim and explain why it should be disregarded.

Core Idea
An objection completely unrelated to the negation of the claim is invalid and must be ignored.

In dialectical debate, not every objection deserves a response. The sixth type covers objections that are entirely unrelated (mubayin) to the claim being debated. Recognizing such objections helps debaters avoid wasting time on irrelevant arguments and maintains focus on the actual point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim and Its Negation
Locate the claimant's position (mudda'a) in the example. Here, the claimant says 'Gog and Magog are invaders, not jinn.' The negation of the claim is 'Gog and Magog are jinn.'
The negation is the direct opposite of what the claimant asserts.
2
Recognize the Mubayin Objection
The objection is mubayin (completely different) from the negation. This means the objector's proposition shares no common ground with the negation. In the source, the objector's proposition is not fully given, but it would be something unrelated to jinn, e.g., 'they are made of clay.'
Mubayin implies no overlap whatsoever; partial overlap belongs to other types.
3
Apply the Ruling
According to the passage, such an objection is not permissible for the objector (la yajuz) and it does not benefit the claimant to refute it. The claimant should simply ignore it.
This saves time and keeps the debate on track.
4
Test with Your Own Example
Construct a new Islamic debate scenario. For instance, if the claim is 'The Sunna is a source of law and not a whim,' the negation is 'the Sunna is a whim.' A mubayin objection would be something like 'The Quran is preserved,' which is completely different from the negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: ما يكون مبايناً لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به والاستناد عليه ولا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بإبطاله. مثاله من اللُّمّي: المعلّل: يأجوج ومأجوج غزاة ليسوا جنّاً.
Sixth: What is completely different from the negation of the prevented claim. It is not permissible for the objector to bring it and rely on it, and it does not benefit the claimant to engage in invalidating it. An example from lam'i: The claimant: Gog and Magog are invaders, not jinn.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type of objection, where the objector's proposition is completely different (mubayin) from the negation of the claim. This means the two concepts have no overlap at all. Such an objection is neither permissible for the objector nor worth refuting for the claimant. The example given is from lam'i reasoning: the claimant says 'Gog and Magog are invaders, not jinn'. The negation of the claim would be 'Gog and Magog are jinn' (since the claim asserts they are not jinn). The objector's proposition (which is not fully provided in the text) would be something completely unrelated to jinn, perhaps 'they are humans' or something mubayin. The example is incomplete on this page, but the rule is clear: irrelevant objections are to be disregarded.

An objection that is completely different from the negation of the claim is invalid and does not require a response.

Invalid Objections: Partial Overlap and Mutual Exclusion
Author explains the fifth and sixth types of objection relationships in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify partial overlap and mutual exclusion relationships between an objection and the negation of a claim in a dialectical exchange, and explain why such objections are invalid.

Core Idea
Partial overlap and mutual exclusion make objections irrelevant in debate.

In Islamic dialectic (adab al-bahth), not every objection is valid. The author classifies relationships between the objection (sabab al-man') and the negated claim (naqid al-da'wa). Two invalid types are partial overlap (umum wa khusus wajhi) and mutual exclusion (tabayun). Understanding these helps the debater avoid wasting time on irrelevant objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and its negation
First, state the arguer's main claim (al-da'wa) and then its logical opposite (naqid al-da'wa). For example, if the claim is 'He who worships idols is a disbeliever', the negation is 'He who worships idols is not a disbeliever'.
2
Locate the questioner's objection
Find the basis of the objection (sabab al-man') given by the questioner. For the same example, the objection is 'he might be wealthy'.
3
Compare the objection to the negated claim
Check the relationship between the objection category and the negated claim category. Do they share some cases but not all? That is partial overlap. Do they exclude each other completely? That is mutual exclusion.
Use set diagrams mentally: partial overlap looks like intersecting circles; mutual exclusion looks like separate circles.
4
Conclude why the objection fails
In both cases, the objection does not directly counter the original claim. It either only partially intersects (so it doesn't disprove the claim for all cases) or is entirely outside (so it misses the point). Therefore, the arguer should not waste time refuting it.
The author explicitly says: 'This is not permissible for the questioner to bring and rely upon, and it does not benefit the arguer to engage in refuting it.'
5
Practice with all examples
Review the examples from probabilistic, certain, and analytic reasoning. For each, identify the claim, negation, objection, and the relationship. Verify that the relationship is either partial overlap or mutual exclusion.
The examples cover all three reasoning types mentioned in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: ما يكون بينه وبين الدعوى الممنوعة عموم وخصوص وجهي، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به والاستناد إليه، ولا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بإبطاله. مثاله من اللُّمّي: المعلّل: الذي يعبد الأوثان كافر. السائل: ممنوع، لمَ لا يكون غنياً؟ (فبين الغني والكافر عموم وخصوص وجهي). مثاله من القطعي: المعلّل: من أعلن الشهادتين فهو داخل في زمرة المسلمين. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهو إنسان؟ (فبين الإنسان وبين من هو داخل في زمرة المسلمين عموم وخصوص وجهي). السادس: ما يكون مبايناً لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة، وهذا لا يجوز للسائل الإتيان به والاستناد عليه ولا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال بإبطاله. مثاله من اللُّمّي: المعلّل: يأجوج ومأجوج غزاة ليسوا جنّاً. السائل: ممنوع، لم لا يكونون إنساً؟. (فبين نقيض الدعوى وسند المنع تباين) النسبة = جن إنس تباين. مثاله من القطعي: المعلّل: نجوم الليل ليست قواعد مسامير دق بها أديم السماء. السائل: ممنوع، كيف وهي أجرام عظيمة ملتهبة تسبح في الفضاء البعيد؟ (فبين نقيض الدعوى وسند المنع تباين) النسبة = هي قواعد مسامير هي أجرام عظيمة ملتهبة تباين. مثاله من الحلّي: المعلّل: عصير العنب هذا ليس خمراً. السائل: ممنوع، ومحلُّ ما ذكرت هو لو أنه كان خلاً. (فبين نقيض الدعوى وسند المنع تباين) النسبة = خمر خل تباين.
The fifth: what is between it and the denied claim is partial overlap (umum wa khusus wajhi). This is not permissible for the questioner to bring and rely upon, and it does not benefit the arguer to engage in refuting it. Its example from probabilistic reasoning: the arguer: 'He who worships idols is an unbeliever.' The questioner: 'Denied; why might he not be wealthy?' (Between 'wealthy' and 'unbeliever' is partial overlap). Its example from certain reasoning: the arguer: 'Whoever declares the two shahadas is included in the group of Muslims.' The questioner: 'Denied; how so, while he is a human?' (Between 'human' and 'he who is included in the group of Muslims' is partial overlap). The sixth: what is mutually exclusive of the negation of the denied claim. This is not permissible for the questioner to bring and rely upon, and it does not benefit the arguer to engage in refuting it. Its example from probabilistic reasoning: the arguer: 'Gog and Magog are raiders, not jinn.' The questioner: 'Denied; why might they not be humans?' (Between the negation of the claim and the basis of the objection is mutual exclusion) The relation = jinn/humans mutual exclusion. Its example from certain reasoning: the arguer: 'The stars of the night are not nails driven into the canopy of the sky.' The questioner: 'Denied; how so, while they are great fiery bodies floating in deep space?' (Between the negation of the claim and the basis of the objection is mutual exclusion) The relation = they are nails driven = they are great fiery bodies mutual exclusion. Its example from analytic reasoning: the arguer: 'This grape juice is not wine.' The questioner: 'Denied; the matter you mentioned is only if it were vinegar.' (Between the negation of the claim and the basis of the objection is mutual exclusion) The relation = wine/vinegar mutual exclusion.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two types of relationships between the objection (sabab al-man') that the questioner (sa'il) brings and the negation of the arguer's claim (naqid al-da'wa). The author states the rule: these two types are invalid in debate. The fifth type is 'umum wa khusus wajhi' (partial overlap), where the objection and the negated claim share some common cases but not all. For example, in probabilistic reasoning, the arguer claims that idol-worshippers are disbelievers; the objection 'he might be wealthy' only partially overlaps with 'disbeliever' because one can be wealthy and a believer, or poor and a disbeliever. Therefore this objection does not properly refute the claim. The sixth type is 'tabayun' (mutual exclusion), where the objection and the negated claim are completely separate. Examples are given from probabilistic, certain, and analytic reasoning. For instance, the claim that Gog and Magog are not jinn is met with the objection that they might be humans; but 'jinn' and 'human' are mutually exclusive categories, so the objection is irrelevant. Similarly, the claim that stars are not nails is met with an objection about fiery bodies, which are mutually exclusive categories. In the analytic example, the claim that grape juice is not wine is met with the objection about vinegar, but wine and vinegar are different substances. The author emphasizes that the arguer should not waste time refuting such objections because they are fundamentally invalid in the rules of debate.

نقيض الدعوى

Checkpoint
In a debate about the ruling on nabidh, the arguer claims: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is wine (khamr).' The opponent objects: 'Nabidh is made from dates, not grapes.' What is the relationship between the objection category 'made from dates' and the negation of the claim 'not wine'?
A) Mutual exclusion (tabayun)
B) Partial overlap (umum wa khusus wajhi)
C) Equivalence (musawa)
D) Absolute specificity (aks mullaq)

Key Takeaway
Partial overlap and mutual exclusion are two invalid objection relationships in dialectic; they do not properly refute the arguer's claim.

Three Valid Responses to a Dialectical Objection
Author presents the three valid methods for the arguer to refute the opponent's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three valid methods for an arguer to refute an opponent's objection in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
Proving the same, equivalent, or more specific claim repels the objection.

In Islamic dialectical theology (jadal), debates follow structured rules. When an opponent (sa'il) denies a claim, the arguer (mu'allil) cannot simply repeat it. Instead, he must bring a proof (dalil) that logically establishes the claim. This lesson unpacks the three valid strategies: proving the exact claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim. These methods ensure that the debate remains rigorous and that objections are addressed with evidence, not repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied claim
Read the objection carefully. What exactly did the sa'il deny? For example, if the mu'allil says 'Nabidh is haram', and the sa'il denies it, then the denied claim is 'Nabidh is haram'.
2
Classify the logical relationship
Determine whether the proof will produce the same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim. Equivalent means co-extensive (same logical scope), more specific means a subset of the original.
The text mentions 'musawi' (equivalent) and 'akhass mutlaq' (absolutely more specific).
3
Construct a proof that fits one method
For direct: prove 'Nabidh is haram' by showing it is an intoxicant and every intoxicant is haram. For equivalent: prove 'Khamr is haram' and argue that khamr is equivalent to nabidh in intoxicating quality. For more specific: prove that a specific type of nabidh, e.g., date-nabidh, is haram, which implies the general claim.
The proof must be valid and sound within the debate's agreed premises.
4
Verify the objection is repelled
If the proof is accepted, the man' is invalidated. Check that the proof directly addresses the objection without introducing new disputes.
The goal is to end the objection, not to create new ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب المعلّل على منع السائل: حين يمنع السائل دعوى أو تصديقاً ما قدمه المعلّل، فللمعلّل في جوابه ودفع منعه ثلاث طرق، وفيما يلي بيانها: الطريق الأولى: أن يأتي بدليل: أ ـ ينتج نفس الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وبذلك يندفع المنع. ب ـ ينتج دعوى أخرى تساوي الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وبذلك يندفع المنع، لأن إثبات مساوي الشيء إثبات له. جـ ـ ينتج دعوى أخرى أخص مطلقاً من الدعوى التي منعها السائل،
The arguer's response to the questioner's objection: When the questioner denies a claim or an assent that the arguer has presented, the arguer has three ways in his answer and his repulsion of the objection, and here is their explanation: The first method: that he brings a proof: a) that produces the very claim which the questioner denied, and thus the objection is repelled. b) that produces another claim equivalent to the claim which the questioner denied, and thus the objection is repelled, because proving the equivalent of something is proving it. c) that produces another claim absolutely more specific than the claim which the questioner denied...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage outlines the three valid strategies for the arguer (mu'allil) to respond when the opponent (sa'il) raises an objection (man') by denying a claim or an assent. The author states that the arguer has three methods, all of which involve bringing a proof (dalil). The first method is to produce a proof that directly yields the exact same claim that was denied; this shows the claim is true and the objection is invalid. The second method is to produce a proof that yields a claim equivalent to the denied claim; since proving the equivalent is tantamount to proving the original, the objection is also repelled. The third method, which is cut off in the text, is to produce a proof that yields a claim that is absolutely more specific (akhass mutlaq) than the denied claim; presumably, by proving a more specific case, the original claim is also established. These methods ensure that the arguer does not simply repeat the claim but provides evidence that addresses the objection directly or through logical equivalence or implication.

Key Takeaway

Three Methods of Responding to Objections in Jadal
Author explains the three methods of response for the proponent in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three methods a proponent can use to counter an opponent's objection in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
Three strategic proofs—same, equal, or more specific—can repel any objection in dialectic.

In a disciplined debate (jadal), the proponent must be ready to defend his claim against the opponent's objection (man'). This passage from the Dawabit al-Ma'rifa presents three powerful methods, each relying on a well-chosen proof. Understanding these methods equips you to construct robust arguments in theological and legal disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and objection
Locate the proponent's claim (da'wa) and exactly what the opponent is objecting to (man'). Also note if the opponent provides a supporting reason (sanad).
In the examples, the objection may be with or without sanad.
2
Classify the proponent's proof
Determine which of the three sub-methods the proponent uses: (a) proof of the same claim, (b) proof of an equal claim, or (c) proof of a more specific claim.
The proof must logically entail the original claim according to the rule given.
3
Verify the logical relationship
Check that the new claim is indeed identical, equal, or absolutely more specific than the original. If equal or more specific, ensure the entailment holds.
Equality means mutual implication; specificity means 'all A are B' but not vice versa.
4
Confirm the objection is repelled
The response works whether the objection has a support or not. The proponent's proof answers the challenge directly.

جواب المعلّل على منع السائل:
حين يمنع السائل دعوى أو تصديقاً ما قدمه المعلّل، فللمعلّل في جوابه ودفع منعه ثلاث طرق، وفيما يلي بيانها:
الطريق الأولى: أن يأتي بدليل:
أ ـ ينتج نفس الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وبذلك يندفع المنع.
ب ـ ينتج دعوى أخرى تساوي الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وبذلك يندفع المنع، لأن إثبات مساوي الشيء إثبات له.
جـ ـ ينتج دعوى أخرى أخص مطلقاً من الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وبذلك يندفع المنع، لأن إثبات الأخص يستلزم إثبات الأعم.
والجواب المنتج لواحد من هذه الاحتمالات الثلاثة، يصلح للرد على المنع المجرد من السند، والمنع المصحوب بالسند كليهما.
أمثلة:
أ ـ المعلّل : هذا الكون حادث (دعوى).
السائل : ممنوع، كيف وهو أزلي قديم؟ (منع مصحوب بسند قطعي).
المعلّل : لقد أثبت القانون الثاني للديناميكا الحرارية أن لهذا الكون بداية، وكل ما له بداية فهو حادث. فهذا الكون حادث (دليل أنتج نفس الدعوى الممنوعة فاندفع المنع).
ب ـ المعلل : مقطّر خمر العنب قاتل للجراثيم (دعوى).
السائل : ممنوع، لم لا يكون غير قاتل للجراثيم؟ (منع مصحوب بسند جوازي).
المعلّل : مقطّر خمر العنب من أنواع الكحول، وكل أنواع الكحول تستعمل في الطب للتعقيم : فمقطر خمر العنب يستعمل في التعقيم (دليل أنتج دعوى تساوي الدعوى الممنوعة ـ باعتبار أن «المعقم» يساوي «قاتل للجراثيم» ـ فاندفع المنع).
جـ ـ المعلل المسلم : عيسى عليه السلام مخلوق لله تعالى .
السائل النصراني : ممنوع، هذا لو لم يكن ابناً لله تعالى . (منع مقرون بسند حلّي).
المعلّل المسلم : عيسى ولدته امرأة من الناس وكان يأكل الطعام، وكل من هذه صفته فهو إنسان : فعيسى عليه السلام إنسان (دليل أنتج دعوى أخص مطلقاً من الدعوى التي منعها السائل، لأن كونه إنساناً أخص مطلقاً من كونه مخلوقاً).
الطريق الثانية: أن يبطل السند الذي استند إليه السائل في المنع، وهذا
Response of the proponent to the opponent's objection:
When the opponent objects to a claim or a proposition that the proponent has presented, the proponent has three ways in his response and repelling of his objection, and here is their explanation:
The first way: that he brings a proof:
A) That produces the same claim that the opponent objected to, and thereby the objection is repelled.
B) That produces another claim equal to the claim that the opponent objected to, and thereby the objection is repelled, because proving the equal of something is proving it.
C) That produces another claim that is absolutely more specific than the claim that the opponent objected to, and thereby the objection is repelled, because proving the more specific necessitates proving the more general.
And the response that produces one of these three possibilities is suitable for replying to both the objection devoid of support and the objection accompanied by support.
Examples:
A) The proponent: This universe is originated (claim).
The opponent: Objection, how so while it is eternal, ancient? (objection accompanied by definite support).
The proponent: The second law of thermodynamics has proven that this universe has a beginning, and everything that has a beginning is originated. So this universe is originated (a proof that produced the same objected claim, so the objection was repelled).
B) The proponent: Distilled grape wine is a germ killer (claim).
The opponent: Objection, why would it not be not a germ killer? (objection accompanied by possible support).
The proponent: Distilled grape wine is a type of alcohol, and all types of alcohol are used in medicine for sterilization: so distilled grape wine is used in sterilization (a proof that produced a claim equal to the objected claim — considering that 'sterilizer' equals 'germ killer' — so the objection was repelled).
C) The Muslim proponent: Jesus, peace be upon him, is a creation of Allah, exalted.
The Christian opponent: Objection, this (would be the case) if he were not the son of Allah, exalted. (objection connected with a conditional support).
The Muslim proponent: Jesus was born by a woman from among people and used to eat food, and everyone with this description is a human: so Jesus, peace be upon him, is a human (a proof that produced a claim absolutely more specific than the claim that the opponent objected to, because his being a human is absolutely more specific than his being a creation).
The second way: that he invalidates the support upon which the opponent relied in the objection, and this
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage describes the dialectical strategies available to the proponent (al-mu'allil) when the opponent (al-sa'il) objects to his claim. The author enumerates three ways of responding to the objection (man'). The first way is to bring a proof (dalil) that can take one of three forms: (a) a proof that directly produces the same claim that was objected to, thereby repelling the objection by reaffirming it with evidence; (b) a proof that produces a claim that is equal (musawi) to the objected claim, such that proving the equal is considered proving the original; (c) a proof that produces a claim that is absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaqan) than the objected claim, because proving the specific entails proving the general. The author notes that any of these three responses works for both an objection without support (sanjad mujarrad) and an objection accompanied by support (man' mas-hub bi-sanad). Three examples are given: the first uses the second law of thermodynamics to prove the universe's origin, directly addressing a definite support; the second shows that distilled grape wine is a sterilizer, which is equal to being a germ killer, repelling a possible support; the third, from Islamic theology, demonstrates that proving Jesus is human (more specific) proves he is created (more general), countering a Christian opponent's conditional support. The passage then introduces the second way—invalidating the opponent's support—but the text is cut off before elaboration.

A) The proponent brings a proof that directly shows zakat is obligatory, e.g., from Quranic text.
B) The proponent brings a proof that zakat is a pillar of Islam, which is equal to being obligatory.
C) The proponent brings a proof that zakat is a specific act of worship (e.g., mentioned in the Quran with specific rules), which is more specific than being obligatory.
D) The proponent invalidates the opponent's support by showing that being a pillar does not negate obligation.

Key Takeaway
A proponent can repel an objection by proving the same claim, an equal claim, or a more specific claim, and these responses work for both supported and unsupported objections.

The First Method: Repelling the Objection by Producing a Counter-Claim
Author explains the first method of repelling an objection with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first method of repelling an objection by producing a new argument that yields a claim equivalent to or more specific than the original denied claim.

Core Idea
To refute an objection, produce a new argument proving something more specific or equal to your original claim.

In jadal, when an objector denies your claim, you can counter by providing a fresh argument that leads to a claim either exactly the same as your original or a more specific version. This works because establishing the more specific automatically establishes the more general, and equivalent claims stand together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim and objection
Read the claimant's original da'wa and the objector's man'. Note whether the objection is bare or accompanied by evidence (sanad).
2
Determine the target of the new argument
Decide whether to produce a claim that is exactly the same as the original (musawi) or more specific (akhass mutlaq). The choice depends on available evidence.
If the new claim is more specific, it must entail the original claim.
3
Construct a new argument
Develop an argument (e.g., syllogism) whose conclusion is the chosen claim. Ensure the premises are sound and acceptable to both parties.
Use Islamic examples: e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
4
Verify logical entailment
Check that the new claim logically implies the original denied claim. For equal claims, they are logically equivalent; for specific claims, the general is entailed.
If entailment fails, the objection is not repelled.
5
Present the argument to repel the objection
Deliver the argument to the objector. If successful, the objector must accept the original claim or provide a new objection.
This method works for both bare objections and those with evidence.

Source Text

And by that the objection is repelled, because proving the more specific necessitates proving the more general. And the answer that produces one of these three probabilities is suitable for responding to both the bare objection (without evidence) and the objection accompanied by evidence. Examples: A – The claimant: This universe is temporally originated (claim). The interlocutor: It is denied; how can it be when it is eternal and ancient? (Objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The claimant: The second law of thermodynamics has proven that this universe has a beginning, and everything that has a beginning is temporally originated. So this universe is temporally originated (evidence that produced the same claim that was denied, so the objection was repelled). B – The claimant: Grape wine distillate kills germs (claim). The interlocutor: It is denied; why could it not be that it does not kill germs? (Objection accompanied by possible evidence). The claimant: Grape wine distillate is a type of alcohol, and all types of alcohol are used in medicine for sterilization: so grape wine distillate is used for sterilization (evidence that produced a claim equal to the denied claim — considering that 'sterilizer' equals 'germ killer' — so the objection was repelled). C – The Muslim claimant: Jesus, peace be upon him, is a created being of Allah. The Christian interlocutor: It is denied; this would be so if he were not the son of Allah (Objection accompanied by a syllogistic evidence). The Muslim claimant: Jesus was born of a woman from among people and used to eat food, and everyone with this description is human: so Jesus, peace be upon him, is human (evidence that produced a claim absolutely more specific than the claim that the interlocutor denied, because his being human is absolutely more specific than his being created). The second method: to invalidate the evidence upon which the interlocutor relied in the objection, and this response is specific to the objection accompanied by evidence. Whenever the evidence is invalidated, the objection is repelled, because the objection is equivalent to the evidence in the view of the objector always(1), so whenever its invalidity appears, the interlocutor has no justification for the objection, because invalidating one of two equivalents is an invalidation of the other, and whenever the objection is invalidated, its negation is established, which is the claimant's original claim, due to the necessity requiring that two contradictories do not combine and do not lift together; so whenever one of them is lifted, the other is established. Examples: A – The claimant: This universe is temporally originated (claim). The interlocutor: I do not accept; why could it not be eternal? (Objection accompanied by possible evidence). The claimant: The second law of thermodynamics has proven that this universe has a beginning, and everything that has a beginning is not eternal: so this universe is not eternal (evidence that invalidated the evidence upon which the interlocutor relied, so its negation was established, which is the claimant's claim). B – The Muslim claimant: Jesus, peace be upon him, is a created being of Allah. The Christian interlocutor: I do not accept; how can that be when he is the son of God? (Objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The Muslim claimant: Jesus was born of a woman from among people, and grew as other people grow, and used to eat food, and anyone with these attributes cannot be the son of God. Then Jesus cannot be the son of God. Or: God is one, eternal, everlasting, and the one, eternal, everlasting cannot have a child. Then God does not have a child. God does not have a child, and everything that does not have a child, Jesus is not his son. Then Jesus is not the son of God. (Two pieces of evidence, each of which invalidated the evidence upon which the interlocutor relied, so the claimant's claim was established). (1) And perhaps it is otherwise in reality and in actual fact.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage explains the two methods for refuting an objection (man') in dialectical debate (jadal). The author first summarizes the first method: the claimant can produce evidence that yields a claim that is equivalent to or more specific than the denied claim, such that proving the specific necessitates the general. The answer must produce one of three types of probability (mentioned earlier in the text). This method works for both bare objections (those without evidence) and objections accompanied by evidence. He then provides three examples: the first uses thermodynamics to prove the universe's temporality, the second uses the equivalence of 'sterilizer' and 'germ killer' to defend grape wine distillate's germ-killing property, and the third uses the specific attribute of being human (which entails being created) to respond to the Christian objection about Jesus being created. Then the author presents the second method: directly invalidating the evidence (sanad) that the objector relied upon. This method applies only to objections with evidence. Once the evidence is invalidated, the objection falls because the objection is equivalent to the evidence in the objector's mind. The author provides two examples: for the universe's origin, the claimant proves it is not eternal (contradicting the objector's evidence); for Jesus, the claimant uses two arguments (one based on his human attributes and another based on God's oneness) to show that Jesus cannot be the son of God, thus invalidating the Christian evidence. The footnote cautions that this equivalence may not hold in actual reality. The commentary should walk the student through each method and example, explaining the logical moves without adding external content.

C) Accepting the objection and modifying the claim.
D) Changing the subject.

Key Takeaway
By producing a new argument that yields a claim more specific or equivalent to the denied claim, the claimant can repel the objection without engaging the objector's evidence.

?

The Second Method: Repelling the Objection by Invalidating the Evidence
Author explains the second method of repelling an objection with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the second method of repelling an objection by directly invalidating the evidence (sanad) upon which the objector relies.

Core Idea
Invalidate the objector's evidence to collapse his objection, because the objection stands on its evidence.

The second method is more direct: you attack the very evidence (sanad) that the objector used to support his denial. Since the objection is tied to that evidence in the objector's view, showing the evidence false kills the objection. This method only works when the objection is accompanied by evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's evidence
Read the objector's man' and extract the supporting evidence (sanad). Determine the type of evidence (definitive, possible, syllogistic).
2
Analyze the evidence for flaws
Examine whether the evidence is logically or factually unsound. Look for false premises, invalid reasoning, or contradictions with known truths.
In Islamic debate, evidence may conflict with revealed knowledge or sound reason.
3
Construct a refutation of the evidence
Develop a counter-argument that directly proves the falsehood or inadequacy of the objector's evidence. This may involve a new syllogism or appeal to accepted principles.
For example, to refute the claim that Jesus is son of God, prove that God cannot have a son.
4
Present the refutation to invalidate the objection
Deliver the counter-argument. Once the evidence is invalidated, the objection collapses because it was equivalent to the evidence in the objector's mind.
The footnote warns that this equivalence may not hold in reality, but it does in the debate context.
5
Establish the original claim by contradiction
After the objection is repelled, the claimant's original claim stands by the law of non-contradiction: since the objection is false, its negation (the original claim) must be true.
This step is automatic once the objection is removed.

Nullifying the Evidence in Dialectical Debate
First method for the arguer to respond to objection by nullifying its evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to execute the method of nullifying the objector's evidence (sanad) in a munazara to defend your original claim.

Core Idea
Nullify the evidence, and the objection falls automatically.

In dialectical debate (munazara), the objector (sa'il) often supports his objection (man') with evidence (sanad). The arguer (mu'allil) cannot simply dismiss the objection; he must address the evidence. The first method taught in this passage is to nullify that evidence. Once the evidence is shown false, the objection collapses, and the original claim is re-established. This lesson walks you through that method using the examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its attached evidence
In the debate, locate where the objector states his objection and any supporting reason. In the first example, the objection is 'I do not accept, why may it not be eternal?' which implicitly relies on the possibility of eternity as evidence.
In jadal, the objector's evidence may be implicit, as in this case.
2
Determine the arguer's strategy to nullify the evidence
Find how the arguer attacks the evidence itself, not just the objection. In the first example, the arguer uses the second law of thermodynamics to show the universe has a beginning, thereby nullifying the notion that it could be eternal.
3
Understand the logical equivalence
Recognize that in the objector's view, the evidence and objection are equivalent. Nullifying one nullifies the other. The text states: 'because the objection is equivalent to the evidence in the view of the objector always.'
This equivalence is crucial; if the evidence is false, the objection loses its support.
4
Apply the principle of non-contradiction
Once the objection is nullified, its opposite (the arguer's claim) is automatically established. The principle that two opposites cannot coexist and cannot both be absent ensures this.
5
Practice with the given examples
Work through both examples: the universe example using thermodynamics, and the Jesus example using rational arguments. Identify the evidence and how each argument nullifies it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب خاص بالمنع المقترن بالسند، ومتى تم إبطال السند اندفع المنع، لأن المنع مساوٍ للسند في نظر المانع دائماً(١)، فمتى ظهر بطلانه لم يكن لدى السائل مبرّر للمنع، لأن إبطال أحد المتساويين هو إبطال للآخر، ومتى بطل المنع ثبت نقيضه، وهي دعوى المعلّل الأصلية، نظراً إلى الضرورة القاضية بأن النقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فمتى ارتفع أحدهما ثبت الآخر. أمثلة : أ ـ المعلل : هذا الكون حادث (دعوى). السائل : لا أسلم، لم لا يكون أزلياً؟ (منع مصحوب بسند جوازي). المعلّل : لقد أثبت القانون الثاني للديناميكا الحرارية أن لهذا الكون بداية، وكل ما له بداية فهو ليس بأزلي : فهذا الكون ليس بأزلي (دليل أبطل السند الذي استند إليه السائل، فثبت نقيضه، وهي دعوى المعلّل). ب ـ المعلّل المسلم : عيسى عليه السلام مخلوق لله تعالى . السائل النصراني : لا أسلم، كيف وهو ابن الله ؟ (منع مقرون بسند قطعي). المعلل المسلم : عيسى ولدته امرأة من الناس، ونما نمو سائر الناس، وكان يأكل الطعام، وكل من هذه صفته فلا يمكن أن يكون ابناً لله . إذن فعيسى لا يمكن أن يكون ابناً لله . أو : الله واحد أزلي أبدي، والواحد الأزلي الأبدي يستحيل أن يكون له ولد. إذن فالله ليس له ولد. الله ليس له ولد، وكل ما ليس له ولد لا يكون عيسى ابنه . إذن فالله لا يكون عيسى ابنه . (دليلان كل منهما أبطل السند الذي استند إليه السائل، فثبتت دعوى المعلّل).(١) وربما يكون بخلاف ذلك في الواقع ونفس الأمر.
The answer is specific to the objection accompanied by evidence. Whenever the evidence is nullified, the objection is repelled, because the objection is equivalent to the evidence in the view of the objector always (1). So when its invalidity becomes apparent, the questioner has no justification for objection, because nullifying one of two equivalents is nullifying the other. When the objection is nullified, its opposite is established, which is the original claim of the arguer, considering the necessity that two opposites cannot coexist and cannot both be absent; when one is removed, the other is established. Examples: A. The arguer: This universe is originated (claim). The questioner: I do not accept, why may it not be eternal? (objection accompanied by permissible evidence). The arguer: The second law of thermodynamics has established that this universe has a beginning, and everything that has a beginning is not eternal; so this universe is not eternal (evidence that nullified the evidence on which the questioner relied, so its opposite is established, which is the claim of the arguer). B. The Muslim arguer: Jesus, peace be upon him, is a creation of Allah Almighty. The Christian questioner: I do not accept, how can he be the son of God? (objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The Muslim arguer: Jesus was born of a woman from humanity, and grew like the rest of humanity, and used to eat food, and everyone with these attributes cannot be a son of God. Therefore Jesus cannot be a son of God. Or: Allah is one, eternal, perpetual; the one, eternal, perpetual cannot have a child. Therefore Allah has no child. Allah has no child, and everyone who has no child, Jesus is not his son. Therefore Allah is not the father of Jesus. (Two pieces of evidence, each of which nullified the evidence on which the questioner relied, so the claim of the arguer was established). (1) Perhaps it is otherwise in reality and actual fact.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage explains the first method for the arguer (mu'allil) to respond to the objector's (sa'il) objection (man') that is accompanied by evidence (sanad). The arguer nullifies the evidence, and since the objection is considered equivalent to the evidence in the objector's view, nullifying the evidence removes the basis for the objection. The principle of non-contradiction ensures that when the objection is nullified, its opposite (the arguer's claim) is established. The two examples illustrate this: the first uses the second law of thermodynamics to show the universe has a beginning, so it is not eternal; the second uses rational arguments to show that Jesus cannot be the son of God.

Key Terms
منع (man')
سند (sanad)
معلّل (mu'allil)
سائل (sa'il)

Checkpoint

A) The second law of thermodynamics
B) The impossibility of an eternal universe
C) He provides no evidence; it is a simple denial
D) The fact that the universe has a beginning

Key Takeaway
To win a debate, do not directly refute the objection; target the evidence it stands on.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Your task is to nullify the evidence that 'it exists in time' by showing how the Quran is eternal as the attribute of speech (kalam nafsi). Write your response step by step.

Clarifying Intended Meaning (Tahrir al-Murad) in Dialectical Dispute
Explains the third method: arguer clarifies his claim by specification, generalization, or convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tahrir al-murad responses in a dialectical exchange and evaluate their validity.

Core Idea
Tahrir al-murad rescues a claim by revealing that the objection misunderstood its intended scope or convention.

In dialectical debates, an objector may reject a claim based on an apparent contradiction. The arguer can respond by clarifying exactly what he meant, showing that the objection does not actually disprove his intended meaning. This lesson unpacks the third method of response: tahrir al-murad, using three concrete examples from Islamic jurisprudence and linguistics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Find the arguer's initial statement (mudda'a) that the objector challenges. Note its wording and apparent scope.
In example (أ), the claim is 'water is not impured by anything except change in color, taste, or odor.'
2
Examine the objection
Determine how the objector interprets the claim and why he denies it. Identify the definitive evidence (dalil qat'i) he provides.
The objector uses the fact that less than two qullas of water becomes impure on contact.
3
Analyze the arguer's clarification
See how the arguer responds by specifying the intended scope: takhsis (narrowing), ta'mim (widening), or appealing to a specific convention or school. Check if the response is consistent with the claim's original wording.
In (أ), he reveals he follows the Maliki school. In (ب), he uses common custom. In (ج), he redefines 'prayer' as supplication.
4
Assess the validity of the response
Evaluate whether the clarification is legitimate according to dialectical rules. A valid tahrir al-murad must not contradict the arguer's earlier position or be a mere evasion. It should reflect a genuine intended meaning that the objector misunderstood.
If the arguer had not intended the Maliki school originally, his response would be invalid. Consistency is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالثة : أن يحرر المعلّل مراده من الدعوى التي منعها السائل، وذلك بتخصيص، أو تعميم، أو جري على اصطلاح خاص، أو جري على مذهب من المذاهب . أمثلة : أ ـ المعلل الفقيه : الماء قليله وكثيره لا ينجسه شيء، إلا ما غلب على لونه، أو طعمه، أو ريحه . السائل : لا أسلم، كيف وما دون القلتين من الماء ينجس بمجرد ملاقاة النجاسة له؟ (منع مقرون بسند قطعي) . المعلّل : لقد جريت فيما ذكرت على مذهب مالك رضي الله عنه . (جواب بتحرير المراد من المذهب الذي جرى عليه). ب ـ المعلّل : كل حيوان ليس له أربعة قوائم فليس بدابة . السائل : ممنوع، كيف والدابة كل ما يدب على الأرض؟ (منع مقرون بسند قطعي) . المعلّل : لقد جريت فيما ذكرت على ما اشتهر في العرف . (جواب بتحرير المراد من الاصطلاح الذي جرى عليه). جـ ـ المعلّل : تجوز الصلاة من غير طهارة . السائل : لا أسلم، كيف وهي لا تجوز إلا بطهارة، باستثناء فاقد الطهورين؟ (منع مقرون بسند قطعي) . المعلّل : أردت من الصلاة مطلق الدعاء، ولم أرد الصلاة الخاصة في الاصطلاح الشرعي . (جواب بتحرير المراد من لفظة الصلاة).
The third way: that the arguer specifies his intended meaning of the claim that the objector objected to, by specification, or generalization, or following a particular convention, or following a particular school of thought. Examples: A. The jurist arguer: Water, its little and much, is not made impure by anything, except what overcomes its color, taste, or odor. The questioner: I do not accept, how is it that water less than two qullas becomes impure simply by contacting impurity? (objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The arguer: I followed in what I mentioned the school of Malik, may Allah be pleased with him. (Answer by clarifying the intended meaning of the school he followed). B. The arguer: Every animal that does not have four legs is not a dābbah (creeping creature). The questioner: Denied, how is it that dābbah is everything that creeps on the earth? (objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The arguer: I followed in what I mentioned what is commonly known in custom. (Answer by clarifying the intended meaning of the convention he followed). C. The arguer: Prayer is permissible without purification. The questioner: I do not accept, how is it that it is not permissible except with purification, except for one who lacks the two purifiers? (objection accompanied by definitive evidence). The arguer: I meant by prayer absolute supplication, and I did not intend the specific prayer in the legal convention. (Answer by clarifying the intended meaning of the word 'prayer').
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage explains the third method for the arguer to respond to an objection: clarifying the intended meaning of his claim. The arguer may specify that he meant a specific category, a general category, followed a specific terminology, or adhered to a particular school. The three examples show this: in the first, the arguer follows the Maliki school regarding water impurity; in the second, he follows common custom in defining 'dābbah'; in the third, he clarifies that he meant 'supplication' not the ritual prayer.

تخصيص

اصطلاح

Key Takeaway
Tahrir al-murad is a legitimate dialectical response when the objector's misunderstanding arises from ambiguous scope or convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Suppose an arguer says: 'Selling dates before they ripen is allowed.' An objector responds: 'But the Prophet forbade selling dates before they ripen!' Avoiding Unhelpful Tactics in Jadal
Lists tactics not beneficial for the arguer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two tactics that do not benefit the arguer in responding to objections, according to the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
Denying the objection or its evidence is a sign of weakness.

In the art of jadal, the arguer must respond to objections with valid reasoning. This passage warns against two common but futile tactics: denying that the objection was properly raised, or denying the evidence supporting it. Recognizing these pitfalls helps you avoid them and strengthen your own dialectical skill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
This passage comes after learning the proper ways to respond to objections. Now the author lists what NOT to do. Read the Arabic carefully to see the two prohibitions.
The passage is incomplete but the key points are clear.
2
First unhelpful tactic: man' sihhat wurud al-man'
This means the arguer tries to deny that the objection was validly made or even occurred. It is a refusal to engage with the objection's substance.
3
Second unhelpful tactic: man' al-sanad
This means denying the evidence that the objector presented. Instead of countering the evidence, the arguer simply dismisses it.
4
Understand why these are unhelpful
Both tactics avoid substantive engagement. They are transparent attempts to evade the argument and undermine the arguer's credibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لا ينفع المعلّل الاشتغال به : إذا عرفنا الطرق التي يجيب بها المعلل على منع السائل، فعلينا أن نعرف أنه لا ينفع المعلّل أن يمنع صحة ورود المنع، ولا أن يمنع السند
What does not benefit the arguer to occupy himself with: When we know the ways by which the arguer responds to the objector's objection, we must know that it does not benefit the arguer to deny the validity of the objection's occurrence, nor to deny the evidence
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage introduces a final note on what the arguer should not do: denying the validity of the objection or denying the evidence itself. It suggests that such tactics are not beneficial. The passage is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
Denying the objection's validity or its evidence are futile tactics; proper jadal requires engaging with the substance.
